## Thread: Direct client req : Sr. Business Analyst at Baltimore, MD a Multiple Years Position. — 9 this month / 12 total

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Direct client req : Sr. Business Analyst at Baltimore, MD a Multiple Years Position.

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-12-01T11:25:27+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9jnoqpFpg4U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<0e52c3a2-fe47-47f7-ba14-1eb170a8cbad@o9g2000vbc.googlegroups.com> <e023df77-7617-47e6-ae53-54c5e3ef3a0b@v29g2000yqv.googlegroups.com>`

```
Ken wrote:
> On Nov 29, 3:28 pm, Vishnu Vardhan <vvard...@netvisionresources.com>
> wrote:
…[46 more quoted lines elided]…
> Ken

This trend has been quietly continuing for years and you are right in what 
you say.

In your case there is no problem with you selling yourself as a SME because 
you have enough experience and vision to perform that role, but what about 
the guys who really only want to program?

I would strongly advise any COBOL guys who are job seeking to forget about 
the traditional divide between Analysts and Programmers  (it isn't really 
recognised any more, partly for the reasons you outlined, but also because 
"IT" is permeating the business and there is expected to be computer skill 
associated with knowledge of the business. Only the most traditional 
bastions of Fortress COBOL would still observe the distinction. The 
perception of IT is based around spreadsheets, databases, and network 
support, not around traditional programming. People don't understand the 
COBOL world and expect it to comply with what they DO understand...)

The days when you could just be a COBOL programmer are pretty much over, 
EXCEPT in Fortress COBOL and the Fortresses are slowly being taken or 
crumbling.

It is actually no bad thing for programmers to get involved with the 
business. At least one layer of mis-communication is removed...:-)

Pete.
```

```
Ken  <klshafer@att.net> wrote:
>On Nov 29, 3:28?pm, Vishnu Vardhan <vvard...@netvisionresources.com>
>wrote:
>> Sr. Business Analyst (Subject Matter Expert)

[snip]

>Hmmmmm... this deserves a close reading from which we can cull out
>some industry trends.

[snip]

>What I am noticing more and more is that COI / Top Management's
>refusal to admit, or take any responsibility for, many bad decisions
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>Internals!

Mr Shafer, our experiences are different; I first noticed this sort of 
thing twenty-some years ago so if it is a trend it certainly has a long 
curve.

The ugly details are... it was at a retailer, IMS DB/DC, and they were 
both trying to implement DB2 and refrain from training their personnel at 
the same time.  They had a more-or-less recurring job posting for a 
programmer, mainframe, COBOL, File-Aid, IMS DB/DC, et and cetera.

One fellow managed, somehow, to get a company-sponsored course in DB2... 
and was promptly hired away by The Competition at a significantly higher 
rate.

The company's response was to tighten up on their no-training policy and 
update the posting to include DB2.

DD
```

```
In article <3f52267e-f6d7-4b9b-92ed-1d38efe53411@v6g2000yqv.googlegroups.com>,
Ken  <klshafer@att.net> wrote:
>On Dec 1, 4:34?pm, docdw...@panix.com () wrote:
>> In article
><e023df77-7617-47e6-ae53-54c5e3ef3...@v29g2000yqv.googlegroups.com>,
>> Ken ?<klsha...@att.net> wrote:

[snip]

>> >This post shows the ultimate absurdity, and the near-impossibility of
>> >educating ?managers... Why, if the projects of the past have failed it
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>Yeah, but Doc, you've ALWAYS been on the bleedin' edge, haven't you?

As much as any COBOL-codin' fool might be, perhaps.

[snip]

>> One fellow managed, somehow, to get a company-sponsored course in DB2...
>> and was promptly hired away by The Competition at a significantly higher
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>to the company another X years or so in return for the DB2 training.

I've heard of that being done and it makes me wonder.  There is a 
technique called 'suicide by cop', where somebody does something so 
outrageous that the police who have been called in to deal with the 
miscreant are permitted by their own rules to shoot-to-kill.  

In that asking the victim 'why did you do this' usually does not result in 
a useful reply the consenus is that said victim wanted to be killed but 
did not want to commit suicide.

Likewise, given the situation you describe of nearly-indentured 
servitude... I wonder what would happen after such an agreement were the 
newly-trained employee begin to 'really speak her mind', especially about 
the Wonderful Ideas of the Corner-Office Idiot.  As far as I know about 
Corporate Life this would almost guarantee a visit from Security to watch 
while you clean out your desk; as far as I know about The Law the company 
would have to forfeit its claim for reimbursement.

>And also
>entirely appropriate for that fellow to counter-propose his salary
>increase at that time. Probably would turn out to be about what they
>had to pay for the up-skilled reposted position.

I also wonder about this.  It might result in a kind of bidding-war 
between employees about who would take the lowest increase.

In a similar light... I was contacted by a pimp about a position recently.  
It looked to be convenient; short commute, The Usual Suspects for 
technology and the rate was 'open'.  I quoted a reasonable rate and was, 
predictably, met with a '*gulp*' and a come-back US$15/hr lower.  I hemmed 
and hawed... and accepted the counter-offer; a few minutes I received an 
e-mail with the rate as 'confirmed'.

Half-an-hour after that I received another email saying 'I know we 
confirmed this rate BUT... I spoke with my boss and the customer and we 
need to go US$6 lower (and even then I'm taking a real cut)'.  My reply 
was along the lines of 'I could not reasonably ask you to do business at a 
loss... let's go on to the next one, shall we?'

Five hours after that I get another email, one from a job-service 
notifying me of a new posting.  It was for the same job discussed above, a 
job that asked for a minimum of 12 years' worth of professional 
experience...

... and offering a rate of US$35 - $40/hr.  At least it demonstrated that 
the pimp was being kind of honest.

DD
```

```
On Dec 8, 8:42 am, docdw...@panix.com () wrote:

I wonder what was the context of the first use of the word
'confirmed'? It may have been
something along of the lines "We agree to submit you at a rate that
would allow us
to pay you this 'confirmed' rate."  It certainly does not seem that it
was confirmed
by the client. Since I did not read of any interview being conducted,
speculation, and
after all it is only speculation, would say that the client response
was
"Hey, I can get any number of vanilla types for $6-$10/hour less."

Once again probably a case of being burned by management's inability
to evaluate talent.

And yet, I wonder, I still wonder, about this proverbial $35-$40/hour
which seems to
be so widespread. In the Olden Dayz that would have been accompanied
by a bill rate
of about $50/hr, because the widely-understood gentlemen's agreement
was a gross
margin of 20-30%.

Some of the pimps are gentlemen, and still adhere to that. Others are,
well, pimps only,
and go for margins of 40-50%, which would put the bill rate at $6-$70/
hr.

Or so I have it from my usual network of (mostly) reliable industry
sources.

It can take something extra to get even a wee bit more, as I was able
to do
recently when I was chosen to replace two heads, since I can support
both
a Honeywell/Bull environment and an aging VSE/ESA environment. This
kind
of advantage has not accrued to me very often.

Doc, did you ever make an historical survey as to how many pimp posts
followed
up with rate info after they read your standard boilerplate? I myself
do not
recall even one. Maybe they don't bother to followup by reading CLC -
they
just wait for the 'flood' of interested e-mails to land in their
Inbox.


Ken
```

```
>Since I did not read of any interview being conducted,

>"Hey, I can get any number of vanilla types for $6-$10/hour less."

My speculation would be that salesfolk of any positive quality respond to 
this with something along the lines of 'Of course you can get any number 
of folks for less but your business is important and you need Somebody 
Good... and *this* guy is Somebody Good.  I can get you someone else for 
less, sure, but both you and I know that you're looking for Real Quality 
and you're going to get what you pay for.'

I also speculate... no, I'll tell you for a fact that I've never made a 
living at sales.

>
>Once again probably a case of being burned by management's inability
>to evaluate talent.

I was not evaluated by a manager, Mr Shafer, and the only evaluation made 
of me was, I believe, by salesfolk reading my bragsheet and saying 'this 
looks good'.

[snip]

>Doc, did you ever make an historical survey as to how many pimp posts
>followed
>up with rate info after they read your standard boilerplate? I myself
>do not
>recall even one.

My memory is, admittedly, porous... but I recall a few folks who have done 
so.  When I post the boilerplate I commit a slight breach of etiquette by 
both posting and emailing; I'll live with that.

The usualy response is no response, second to that is responding by email 
as to how there are Very Good Reasons for not posting rates, next is 
emailing me the rate, next is emailing me the rate and asking that I post 
it, least common of all is another posting containing the rates.

That last response is usually followed by derisive postings about the size 
of the rate.

> Maybe they don't bother to followup by reading CLC -
>they
>just wait for the 'flood' of interested e-mails to land in their
>Inbox.

The Olde Knowledge is 'everyone wants a twenty-year-old virgin who knows 
everything that a forty-year-old hooker does'... and everyone seems to 
want to pay fresh-out-of-college rates for decades of experience.

As the Germans say, 'c'est la guerre'.

DD
```

```
>On Dec 1, 4:34ï¿½pm, docdw...@panix.com () wrote:
>> In article <e023df77-7617-47e6-ae53-54c5e3ef3...@v29g2000yqv.googlegroups.com>,

Back in the dark ages when we used to write code via candlelight and
type said code into 80 column cards, my employer offered classes in a
new and exciting technique on the IBM Mainframe known as CICS.  They
offered, and I took, CICS Command Level Programming.  They then
offered, and I took, a two week course in sunny Marina Del Rey
California at the training headquarters of Online Software.  The
course was CICS internals.  

With this new knowledge, I became the onsite (at my shop) CICS System
Programmer which fit in nicely with my other application and VSE/VM
System responsibilities.

For the pleasure of an extra 20 or so hours of work a week, I was not
forced to commit to working for the company any longer than I wanted
to.   I'm pretty sure had that been a condition of getting those
classes, I would have done it, but it wasn't so I didn't.

Regards,
```

```
On Dec 1, 9:51 am, "HeyBub" <hey...@NOSPAMgmail.com> wrote:
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
> > As for "practically every other gimmick..."... this is a meaningless
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: INFOWORLD: Programming languages on the rise: Cobol

- **From:** Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-12-03T22:17:16-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5b64ff68-01dc-4b2b-b52a-e049e0efd412@l19g2000yqc.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<25588436.224.1322504387166.JavaMail.geo-discussion-forums@vbxl10> <9jiqosFaqpU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Nov 28, 8:27 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>  Please cite ONE single "gimmick" that has
> been added to COBOL in the last 20 years (and actually implemented by a
> COBOL vendor) and is NOT Object based.

Depends on how one interprets "gimmick", "added to COBOL", and
"actually implemented".

For example, floating-point was "added to COBOL" in the 2002
standard, but was "actually implemented" by COBOL vendors
before the revision to the standard, and it could be considered
a "gimmick" because floating-point is normally scientific use,
not business use.

Another example includes native binary support, and data and
procedure pointers. These are related to systems programming,
rather than business programming.

None of these is object based.

However, this cannot satisfy your challenge, because you asked
for "ONE" and I supplied two. <g>
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: INFOWORLD: Programming languages on the rise: Cobol

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **References:** `<25588436.224.1322504387166.JavaMail.geo-discussion-forums@vbxl10> <9jiqosFaqpU1@mid.individual.net> <5b64ff68-01dc-4b2b-b52a-e049e0efd412@l19g2000yqc.googlegroups.com> <9saod7lq2kfdo2am1v2lirlp39ffrq0u7r@4ax.com> <_NudnVzCdpMJ_kHTnZ2dnUVZ5vadnZ2d@giganews.com> <20138745.6.1323212919303.JavaMail.geo-discussion-forums@prgg19>`

```
On 12/6/2011 5:08 PM, Frank Swarbrick wrote:
> On Sunday, December 4, 2011 11:24:19 PM UTC-7, Arnold Trembley wrote:
>> On 12/4/2011 8:27 PM, Clark F Morris wrote:
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
> Frank

Frank,

I was only told that dynamic call via Procedure-Pointer was faster than 
dynamic call by name.  I haven't run any tests to prove or disprove that.

I created a tiny program to see what code is generated:


01  800-PTR-SI994010     EXTERNAL       PROCEDURE-POINTER.
01  300-LITERALS.
     05  300-SI994010            PIC X(08) VALUE 'SI994010'.
     05  300-SI995010            PIC X(08) VALUE 'SI995010'.

SET 800-PTR-SI994010        TO ENTRY 300-SI994010.



040  SET
0003F0  D207 D150 A176          MVC   336(8,13),374(10)       TS2=0
0003F6  DC07 D150 A022          TR    336(8,13),34(10)        TS2=0
0003FC  4130 A20E               LA    3,526(0,10)             PGMLIT AT +510
000400  5030 D160               ST    3,352(0,13)             TS3=0
000404  58F0 20F8               L     15,248(0,2)             V(IGZCPPL )
000408  4110 A182               LA    1,386(0,10)             PGMLIT AT +370
00040C  0DEF                    BASR  14,15


The SET command is executed only once, during program initialization.
Here is what the CALL looks like:

     PERFORM 3000-CALL-SI994010.

3000-CALL-SI994010.
     CALL 800-PTR-SI994010.

041  PERFORM
044  *3000-CALL-SI994010
045  CALL
00040E  1B11                    SR    1,1
000410  5830 9134               L     3,308(0,9)              BLX=0
000414  58F0 3000               L     15,0(0,3) 
800-PTR-SI994010
000418  4100 3004               LA    0,4(0,3) 
800-PTR-SI994010+4
00041C  58C0 9080               L     12,128(0,9)             TGTFIXD+128
000420  0DEF                    BASR  14,15
000422  58C0 90E8               L     12,232(0,9)             TGTFIXD+232
000426  5840 9128               L     4,296(0,9)              BL=1
00042A  40F0 4000               STH   15,0(0,4)               RETURN-CODE

The CALL would be executed multiple times.


Now here is the dynamic call using program name in a variable:

     PERFORM 5000-CALL-SI995010.

5000-CALL-SI995010.
     CALL 300-SI995010.


042  PERFORM
046  *5000-CALL-SI995010
047  CALL
00042E  D207 D150 A16E          MVC   336(8,13),366(10)       TS2=0
000434  DC07 D150 A022          TR    336(8,13),34(10)        TS2=0
00043A  D203 D158 A17E          MVC   344(4,13),382(10)       TS2=8
000440  4150 D150               LA    5,336(0,13)             TS2=0
000444  5050 D15C               ST    5,348(0,13)             TS2=12
000448  4110 D158               LA    1,344(0,13)             TS2=8
00044C  58F0 2100               L     15,256(0,2)             V(IGZCFCC )
000450  0DEF                    BASR  14,15
000452  40F0 4000               STH   15,0(0,4)               RETURN-CODE
043  GOBACK
000456  5850 9148               L     5,328(0,9)              IPCB=1+16


So one instruction path is 32 bytes long and has no Storage-to-Storage 
instructions, and the other path is 40 bytes long and has three 
Storage-to-storage instructions.

It's possible that over a few hundred million iterations there would be 
a noticeable performance difference.  But I would have to test it a 
number of times to be certain.

With Kindest regards,
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: INFOWORLD: Programming languages on the rise: Cobol

- **From:** Frank Swarbrick <fswarbrick@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-12-06T15:06:23-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1162730.14.1323212783541.JavaMail.geo-discussion-forums@prez5>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<5b64ff68-01dc-4b2b-b52a-e049e0efd412@l19g2000yqc.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<25588436.224.1322504387166.JavaMail.geo-discussion-forums@vbxl10> <9jiqosFaqpU1@mid.individual.net> <5b64ff68-01dc-4b2b-b52a-e049e0efd412@l19g2000yqc.googlegroups.com>`

```
By "native binary support" do you refer to USAGE BINARY-SHORT and it's like, or the "bit twiddling" things, or both?
I consider both to be worthy additions.  The BINARY-SHORT etc. usages because many APIs use actual full-word/half-word etc. fields.  For example, a CICS COMMAREA can be up to 32767 bytes (or so; maybe a bit less), but if you use PIC S9(4) COMP you can only use up to 9999 bytes (without playing games).  Or you can use COMP-5 on z/OS, but that's an extension; one that wouldn't be required if they supported BINARY-SHORT.

As for bit twiddling, we have (for better or worse!) some current data where we pack several 'flag' fields in to a single "binary" character field.  We have to use assembler subroutines to convert from "bits to bytes" and "bytes to bits".  So directly supported bit twiddling would be nice here.

As for floating point, I've never used it in COBOL, and doubt I will need to any time soon.  So you've still got your one.  :-)

Frank
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: INFOWORLD: Programming languages on the rise: Cobol

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-12-06T16:01:53-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7158c511-9d3e-4e53-85bd-c04e26562632@13g2000vbu.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<25588436.224.1322504387166.JavaMail.geo-discussion-forums@vbxl10> <9jiqosFaqpU1@mid.individual.net> <5b64ff68-01dc-4b2b-b52a-e049e0efd412@l19g2000yqc.googlegroups.com> <1162730.14.1323212783541.JavaMail.geo-discussion-forums@prez5>`

```
On Dec 6, 6:06 pm, Frank Swarbrick <fswarbr...@gmail.com> wrote:
> By "native binary support" do you refer to USAGE BINARY-SHORT and it's like, or the "bit twiddling" things, or both?

Both, or comprehensively, USAGEs
BINARY-CHAR [ SIGNED | UNSIGNED ]
BINARY-SHORT [ SIGNED | UNSIGNED ]
BINARY-LONG [ SIGNED | UNSIGNED ]
BINARY-DOUBLE [ SIGNED | UNSIGNED ]
BIT
FLOAT-SHORT
FLOAT-LONG
FLOAT-EXTENDED
POINTER [ TO type-name-1 ]
PROGRAM-POINTER [ TO program-prototype-name-1 ]

> I consider both to be worthy additions.  The BINARY-SHORT etc. usages because many APIs use actual full-word/half-word etc. fields.  For example, a CICS COMMAREA can be up to 32767 bytes (or so; maybe a bit less), but if you use PIC S9(4) COMP you can only use up to 9999 bytes (without playing games).  Or you can use COMP-5 on z/OS, but that's an extension; one that wouldn't be required if they supported BINARY-SHORT.
>
> As for bit twiddling, we have (for better or worse!) some current data where we pack several 'flag' fields in to a single "binary" character field.  We have to use assembler subroutines to convert from "bits to bytes" and "bytes to bits".  So directly supported bit twiddling would be nice here.

I don't understand "We have to use assembler subroutines ..."
since COBOL 85 (with intrinsic functions) can handle that in
a "standard conforming" way. <g>

> As for floating point, I've never used it in COBOL, and doubt I will need to any time soon.  So you've still got your one.  :-)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: INFOWORLD: Programming languages on the rise: Cobol

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-12-06T19:27:07-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9cfd7653-c8d4-44d0-818a-ea6e3868c7d8@q11g2000vbq.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<25588436.224.1322504387166.JavaMail.geo-discussion-forums@vbxl10> <9jiqosFaqpU1@mid.individual.net> <5b64ff68-01dc-4b2b-b52a-e049e0efd412@l19g2000yqc.googlegroups.com> <1162730.14.1323212783541.JavaMail.geo-discussion-forums@prez5> <7158c511-9d3e-4e53-85bd-c04e26562632@13g2000vbu.googlegroups.com> <1663010.47.1323218590660.JavaMail.geo-discussion-forums@pret21> <e11a0937-b81d-4d12-a539-a918981a71dd@g7g2000vbd.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Dec 6, 8:44 pm, Rick Smith <rs847...@gmail.com> wrote:
> On Dec 6, 7:43 pm, Frank Swarbrick <fswarbr...@gmail.com> wrote:
> > On Tuesday, December 6, 2011 5:01:53 PM UTC-7, Rick Smith wrote:

[snip]

> > > I don't understand "We have to use assembler subroutines ..."
> > > since COBOL 85 (with intrinsic functions) can handle that in
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> tomorrow.

Bit patterns are for ASCII chracters.
=====
       identification division.
       program-id. bits.
       data division.
       working-storage section.
       1 chr global pic x.
       1 bit-array global.
        2 occurs 8.
         3 bit-value pic 9.
       1 saved-values.
        2 occurs 5.
         3 saved-value pic x(8).
       procedure division.
       begin.
           display quote "chr2bit" quote
           perform test-chr2bit
           display space
           display quote "bit2chr" quote
           perform test-bit2chr
           stop run
           .

       test-chr2bit.
           move "F" to chr
           call "chr2bit"
           move bit-array to saved-value (1)
           display chr space bit-array
           move "r" to chr
           call "chr2bit"
           move bit-array to saved-value (2)
           display chr space bit-array
           move "a" to chr
           call "chr2bit"
           move bit-array to saved-value (3)
           display chr space bit-array
           move "n" to chr
           call "chr2bit"
           move bit-array to saved-value (4)
           display chr space bit-array
           move "k" to chr
           call "chr2bit"
           move bit-array to saved-value (5)
           display chr space bit-array
           .

       test-bit2chr.
           move saved-value (1) to bit-array
           call "bit2chr"
           display bit-array space chr
           move saved-value (2) to bit-array
           call "bit2chr"
           display bit-array space chr
           move saved-value (3) to bit-array
           call "bit2chr"
           display bit-array space chr
           move saved-value (4) to bit-array
           call "bit2chr"
           display bit-array space chr
           move saved-value (5) to bit-array
           call "bit2chr"
           display bit-array space chr
           .

       identification division.
       program-id. chr2bit.
       data division.
       working-storage section.
       77 int binary pic 9(4).
       procedure division.
       begin.
           compute int = function ord (chr) - 1
           compute bit-value (1) = function mod
               (function integer (int / 128) 2)
           compute bit-value (2) = function mod
               (function integer (int / 64) 2)
           compute bit-value (3) = function mod
               (function integer (int / 32) 2)
           compute bit-value (4) = function mod
               (function integer (int / 16) 2)
           compute bit-value (5) = function mod
               (function integer (int / 8) 2)
           compute bit-value (6) = function mod
               (function integer (int / 4) 2)
           compute bit-value (7) = function mod
               (function integer (int / 2) 2)
           compute bit-value (8) = function mod
               (int 2)
           exit program
           .
       end program chr2bit.

       identification division.
       program-id. bit2chr.
       data division.
       working-storage section.
       77 int binary pic 9(4).
       procedure division.
       begin.
           compute int = bit-value (1) * 128
               + bit-value (2) * 64
               + bit-value (3) * 32
               + bit-value (4) * 16
               + bit-value (5) * 8
               + bit-value (6) * 4
               + bit-value (7) * 2
               + bit-value (8)
           move function char (int + 1) to chr
           exit program
           .
       end program bit2chr.
       end program bits.
=====
"chr2bit"
F 01000110
r 01110010
a 01100001
n 01101110
k 01101011

"bit2chr"
01000110 F
01110010 r
01100001 a
01101110 n
01101011 k
=====
```

```
Pete,
  With the exception of the table SORT featrue, I can't believe that ANY of 
the compilers that you have access to don't "correctly" handle ODOs for
 - All subscript
 - SEARCH ALL

I certainly know that Micro Focus, Fujitsu and IBM (mainframe and iSeriec) 
all CORRECTLY handle (use the current number of elments) for ODO's for these 
constructs.

Now, you still may not want to use ODO's as they do NOT save storage and 
they do (sometimes) add performance issues, but they do provide a "pgrammer 
function" that is not (easily) handled otherwise.  (the use of low-value 
entries at the begging or high-value at the end of xied length tables, works 
with SEARCH ALL but does NOT work with the "ALL" subscriptfor most intrinsic 
funcitons)
```

```
BOUND checking is a feature (that can be turned on or off) in the current 
ANSI/ISO Standard.  It is implmented by (some, but not many) COBOL 
implmentors

Look fo the
  EC-RANGE
featgure of the Common Exception facility.

Many (most?) implentors did provide extensions for this before the Standard 
defined this.  (e.g. Micro Focus BOUNDCHECK and IBM SSRANGE).  These 
features also detect reference modificaiton out-of-bound situations (and can 
be turned on or off at run-time so minimal performance hits exist for 
"production" code with "validated" data)
   "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:417dd64b-6273-4dd5-bb71-954cb61fc286@n6g2000vbg.googlegroups.com...
On Nov 29, 9:26 pm, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
>
> >> Actually usually Cobol has little more control over abuse of indexes
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> Hardly. If subscript range checks are a feature of the language,

Are they, in fact, a feature of the _language_ ?

> they
> probably need to be implemented by the compiler. With most Cobol
> compilers set to "optimize," they're not.

Bound checking is a debugging tool available to the programmer and is
not a requirement of the language. With some compilers the optimise
option and the bound check option are separate and independent. Of
course bound checking incurs an overhead.

> So you can't claim that
> (say) C is worse because it doesn't have range checks, if your running
> you Cobol code without the range checks turned on.

I don't make claims about languages being 'worse' or 'better'. They
are different because they fulfill different needs.

> And the notion
> that you only have them on during development is silly - almost all of
> the buffer overflows that happen to C programs didn't happen during
> testing, just with peculiar, often malicious, input data.

You seem unaware of proper development procedures and coding
techniques. Bound checks are there to demonstrate that adequate
checking has been done by the programmer, not to replace those checks.


> While not impacting the sixties and seventies, it's now pretty clear
> that you can optimize much of the work of range checks away (obviously
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> them happening).

What happens in the C language is irrelevant to COBOL, or indeed to
FORTRAN.


> >> But the point was that the Fortran guys (initially) restricted the
> >> forms of the allowed subscript expression in order to help
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> See above.

Which is just more of your waffle and not a mechanism.


> >> >> >In any case FORTRAN only had arrays of numbers (int, real, double,
> >> >> >etc) and abused them by storing text strings across array elements.
…[26 more quoted lines elided]…
> Fortran than Cobol).

And you still haven't demonstrated, merely asserted, where and how
that is superior.

You could, for example, provide code examples comparing what a FORTRAN
compiler has generated and an optimising COBOL compiler has, and why.

But no, you merely claim that COBOL compiler writers are apparently
unaware of what other languages do.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 77 Levels

```
In general the optimization in the IBM 370/390/z series COBOL
compilers starting with COBOL II V4 on thru Enterprise COBOL V4 has
been quite good for the most part.  Constructs that I avoided in
predecessors became the way to do things.  Perform overhead went down
especially with code movement.  Subscripting also was optimized so
that the subscript wasn't recalculated for each use if the compiler
could determine the value hadn't changed.  I have other beefs with the
COBOL team (including the abysmal handling of TRUNC(BIN)) but in
general the compiler is good at optimization.

Clark Morris
```

```
On Dec 4, 9:22 pm, Clark F Morris <cfmpub...@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote:
> On Mon, 28 Nov 2011 23:06:59 -0600, Robert Wessel
>
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> general the compiler is good at optimization.

Mr Wessel's complaint was temporally anomalous! The specific
optimization technique known as "strength reduction" was
discovered circa 1977 and became generally applicable to
COBOL with the addition of in-line PERFORM in 1985. It may
also applie to the movement of code for out-of-line PERFORMs
to make them equivalent to in-line PERFORMSs. But it was NOT
available when indexing was implemented for compilers based
on the 1965 revision.

For that matter, it was NOT available for "the FORTRAN guys"
until 1977!

See < http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strength_reduction >
for a description of the technique.
```

```
On Dec 5, 4:35 pm, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
> On Mon, 5 Dec 2011 10:30:17 -0800 (PST), Rick Smith
> <rs847...@gmail.com> wrote:
…[27 more quoted lines elided]…
> call it that.

I, respectfully, withdraw my comments regarding FORTRAN.

>
> And you're using, as an excuse, the fact that Cobol didn't have a
> proper looping construct until '85?!  Seriously?  Have you guys ever
> heard the story of the Genius Tailor?

Apology, elsewhere, noted.

After my prior post, I did some sleuthing regarding John Cocke
whose 1977 paper is referenced in the cited wikipedia article.
See also < http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Cocke >.
=====
John Cocke (May 30, 1925 – July 16, 2002) was an American
computer scientist recognized for his large contribution to
computer architecture and optimizing compiler design. He is
considered by many to be "the father of RISC architecture."

Cocke spent his entire career as an industrial researcher
for IBM, from 1956 to 1992.
=====

Looking for Cocke in other sources, I tracked the idea of
"strength reduction" for arrays to 1972 and stopped at some
references to papers by others from 1969.

Though I could not find references to the early '60s, I am
willing to accept that indexing ("strength reduction" for
arrays) was known at that time. But, if it was a known
technique for FORTRAN arrays, then it was a known technique
for COBOL arrays. That being the case, then FORTRAN-style
indexing must have been rejected as inappropriate for COBOL.

A plausible reason for such a rejection was that application
to COBOL would likely have required global analysis of the
procedure division -- something not applicable to FORTRAN
and not easily done with "core" measured in kilobytes.
Using index-names provides the benefits of indexing without
requiring global analysis!

I still hold that the earliest FORTRAN-style indexing could
have been applied to COBOL was the 1985 standard. If the
compiler, when processing an in-line perform, can determine
that a [data-name as a] subscript will not change or be
saved during the loop, then it can apply indexing.
```

```
On Mon, 5 Dec 2011 19:06:25 -0800 (PST), Rick Smith
<rs847925@gmail.com> wrote:

>On Dec 5, 4:35ï¿½pm, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
>> On Mon, 5 Dec 2011 10:30:17 -0800 (PST), Rick Smith
…[74 more quoted lines elided]…
>saved during the loop, then it can apply indexing.


I think we could fruitlessly debate what optimization could have been
implemented for which compiler, and when, for a very long time.

Even if you applied only a fairly local analysis, many cases would be
covered as the same (or nearby) array elements are often accessed
multiple times within a paragraph, and that sort of subscript
reference could easily be optimized (although that would properly be
common subexpression elimination, rather than strength reduction).

Although I'd argue that adding a proper looping construct would have
been a far better use of everybody's time than indexes.

My complaint about Cobol indexes is less the INDEXED BY clause, and
more the absurdly separate method of manipulating them.

If you want to provide an optimization hint (I'd take a skeptical
stance about the hint being useful in the long term*, although in the
context of the early sixties, I'd certainly grant some slack), a
dedicated "INDEXED BY" variable is not unreasonable (that does miss
the important case where you want to access two tables with the same
subscript).  OTOH, reversing it makes some sense too (IOW, allow a
numeric field to say "INDEXES TABLE-1").

But it should at least be an ordinary numeric variable for all other
purposes.


*Optimization hints in languages are more notable for being cruft to
work around over time, rather than being generally useful (which is
not to say there aren't counterexamples), so making them fairly
ignorable is generally a good idea.
```

```
My understanding is that Microfocus, for one, has done this for

In what way is COBOL's looping not 'proper' except in your own
degraded definition of 'proper' (which seems to be 'how FORTRAN does
it').

Personally I find that having to use terminating labels rather than
blocks (paragraphs or terminated scope) is less than 'proper'.


> My complaint about Cobol indexes is less the INDEXED BY clause, and
> more the absurdly separate method of manipulating them.

If you don't like it then don't use it. It is not compulsory to use

indexes and then advocate an absurdly separate method for declaring
indexes. Allowing a field to be declared as an index separately from
the table makes no sense, it would allow a physical separation of the
declarations which is prevented by declaring the index(es) as part of
the table.

There is USAGE INDEX, so separate indexes can be declared.

> But it should at least be an ordinary numeric variable for all other
> purposes.

Just use subscripts then. Your suggestion is like suggesting that
pointers in C should be able to be used as numeric values.


> *Optimization hints in languages are more notable for being cruft to

> not to say there aren't counterexamples), so making them fairly
```

```
On Mon, 5 Dec 2011 21:40:28 -0800 (PST), Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>

>> Although I'd argue that adding a proper looping construct would have

>blocks (paragraphs or terminated scope) is less than 'proper'.


Well, given that nobody else has chosen to do it the old Cobol way,
and that separating the bodies and controls for loops is often a
rather bad idea in terms of clarity...  And we were clearly discussing
pre-85 Cobol in this regard.

And a named label as the end of loop is pretty ugly too, but at least
the loop body is inline.


>> My complaint about Cobol indexes is less the INDEXED BY clause, and
>> more the absurdly separate method of manipulating them.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>+=1, pointless.


Of course they don't do the same thing.  And many people have issues
with the side effects that the post and pre-increment operators
introduce as they impact the understandability of C programs, although
they do allow certain things to be expressed quite concisely (which is
one of my major beefs with Cobol indexes - they help nothing in that
regard).


>> If you want to provide an optimization hint (I'd take a skeptical
>> stance about the hint being useful in the long term*, although in the
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>indexes.


One is a tag on a data item to be used as an opimization hint by the
compiler, the other requires wholesale changes throughout the program
to use.


> Allowing a field to be declared as an index separately from
>the table makes no sense, it would allow a physical separation of the
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>pointers in C should be able to be used as numeric values.


C pointers are not like Cobol indexes.  You *cannot* do the equivalent
of "SET subscript TO index" in C, without reference to the base of the
array that the pointer points into.  OTOH, you can mechanically (but
tediously) scatter "SET temp-subscript TO index" and "SET index TO
temp-subscript" statements around the call and then manipulate temp
-subscript with the conventional statements.  Why can't the compiler
at least do that for me?


>> *Optimization hints in languages are more notable for being cruft to
>> work around over time, rather than being generally useful (which is
>> not to say there aren't counterexamples), so making them fairly
>> ignorable is generally a good idea.
```

```
On Dec 6, 9:12 pm, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
> On Mon, 5 Dec 2011 21:40:28 -0800 (PST), Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz>
> wrote:
>

> >> Although I'd argue that adding a proper looping construct would have
> >> been a far better use of everybody's time than indexes.
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> pre-85 Cobol in this regard.

Whether it improves clarity or reduces it is a matter of opinion, and
entirely what one is used to. In 'Structured Programming' terms (eg
Jackson) taking the bodies out of line improves the clarity of the
higher level structure.

> And a named label as the end of loop is pretty ugly too, but at least
> the loop body is inline.

It would be possible to write a complete large program entirely in-
line, starting at the first line and looping and dropping through to
the last. This would not be the best way to write large programs.
Separating the program into layers improves clarity of the structure,
as well as allowing reuse. FORTRAN and COBOL do this differently.

The FORTRAN language was developed to aid the compiler writer to write
efficient machine code. The COBOL language was developed to aid the
application programmer.


> >> My complaint about Cobol indexes is less the INDEXED BY clause, and
> >> more the absurdly separate method of manipulating them.
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> regard).

You are confused, COBOL was not intended to be concise.

If you want concise then use APL.

> >So you complain that there is an "absurdly separate method" for
> >indexes and then advocate an absurdly separate method for declaring
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> to use.

Indexes do _not_ "require wholesale changes". Subscripts still work in
existing programs, there is no _requirement_ for any change. As
indexes have been around for over 40 years then programs will have
been written with one or the other or both.

You appear to be obsessed with a crusade of forcing others to change
how their programs work.

If you wish to write your own language that is slightly different from
COBOL then please do so and let anyone who wishes follow you. If you
want to use FORTRAN then you can do that too (though you may find
difficulty in finding remuneration).

It is far too late to get COBOL made to be more like FORTRAN, that was
for the 1950s.

> > Allowing a field to be declared as an index separately from
> >the table makes no sense, it would allow a physical separation of the
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> at least do that for me?

No one cares. C programmers don't swap pointers and subscript values.
If you want the subscript value in COBOL then don't use indexes, or
increment a numeric item whenever you increment the index.

You have this hate of 'SET', then don't use it.
```

```
Not a crusade, but to the extent that computing would be improved by
the use of better tools, I'd certainly encourage that.  Cobol is a bad
tool (the standard has certainly improved in recent years - it's still
not good, but rather less horrible - but that's been pretty irrelevant
to the real world).  It was an interesting experiment when we didn't
know any better, but it clearly failed in its purpose to allow
non-programmers access to code.  And it was specifically designed
without much consideration for professional programmers (see Grace
Hopper).  

But Cobol provided a number of things valuable to the data processing
(as opposed to CS) communities, scaled decimal arithmetic being a
large, and obvious example.  I've certainly (and publically*) placed a
fair bit of blame on the CS community for Cobol's extended life -
their refusal to deal with requirements like currency calculations in
"good" languages left many people without real alternatives. Defending
Cobol as a whole because it provide those things, OTOH, is silly.

…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>for the 1950s.

late 50s, and made many mistakes, some unfixable, other not.  Even
many of the fixable ones were not fixed until much, much later.  And
many of the things which were done, indexes, for example, or not done
inline loops, types, for example, are difficult to justify as having
and sort of coherency.


>> > Allowing a field to be declared as an index separately from
>> >the table makes no sense, it would allow a physical separation of the
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
>You have this hate of 'SET', then don't use it.


I don't hate it - I find it silly.  You, on the other hand, seem
excessively fond of it.
```

```
On Tue, 6 Dec 2011 15:24:02 -0800 (PST), Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
wrote:

>On Dec 7, 8:18ï¿½am, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
>> On Tue, 6 Dec 2011 10:13:35 -0800 (PST), Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz>
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
>stuff out of line and stick it somewhere else.


Modest sized loops are usually clearer if coded inline.  And the vast
majority of loops are short.


>> >> And a named label as the end of loop is pretty ugly too, but at least
>> >> the loop body is inline.
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
>to exclude COBOL CALL ?


Apparently we'll have to disagree about the near unusability of old
style Cobol CALLs for subroutines.
 

>> >The FORTRAN language was developed to aid the compiler writer to write
>> >efficient machine code. The COBOL language was developed to aid the
…[38 more quoted lines elided]…
>beefs with Cobol indexes - they help nothing in that regard)."


My position, which should be obvious, even if you disagree with it, is
that Cobol indexes actually are negatives in terms of expressing a
problem, and fairly clearly not necessary at all, even for performance
reasons.  And indexes add no expressiveness.  Contrast, for example,
SEARCH ALL, which implements a slightly-tricky-to-get-right algorithm
in a very concise way (albeit still rather wordy from the perspective
of most languages).


>> Nor is wordiness equivalent to clarity.
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>that simply through lack of usage and familiarity ?

By that standard, we'd never have invented HLLs at all!

But one more time.  Using indexes, leads to more code (statements,
words, typing, reading) to express the same concept, while adding a
number of non-trivial rules that need to obeyed regarding their use.
While adding nothing to you're ability to express the problem.  This
is unlikely to lead to clarity.


>> >> >So you complain that there is an "absurdly separate method" for
>> >> >indexes and then advocate an absurdly separate method for declaring
…[29 more quoted lines elided]…
>in the 60s.


And just what relevance do the Cobol standards after '85 have in the
real world?

>> non-programmers access to code.
>
>It was far better at this than any other contemporary language.


Sure.  Cobol got 2% of the way to that goal, and no other contemporary
general purpose language got past .1%.  So it failed utterly at that
goal while trouncing its competitors.


>> And it was specifically designed
>> without much consideration for professional programmers (see Grace
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>doing for performance reasons.


I was quoting (summarized, at least) Grace Hopper, for crying out
loud...


>> But Cobol provided a number of things valuable to the data processing
>> (as opposed to CS) communities, scaled decimal arithmetic being a
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>I'm not surprised.


This becomes tedious...

A major reason Cobol was heavily used was good support of certain
functions near-and-dear to business programmers.  Languages I'd argue
are clearly better (Algol, for example), lacked some of those
features, and were often dismissed by the DP community as unsuitable
for their use, with some justification.  The CS community, OTOH,
largely refused to their pretty little languages with nasty little
things like scaled decimal arithmetic.  The current attitude of
developers of Java, C#, and similar is markedly different.  Instead
much of the CS community looked down their noses at the DP community,
said things like "the use of Cobol cripples the mind," and continued
to ignore their needs.   The DP community largely returned the favor
and attitude, and largely dismissed "all that ivory tower stuff."


>> >If you wish to write your own language that is slightly different from
>> >COBOL then please do so and let anyone who wishes follow you. If you
…[54 more quoted lines elided]…
>them.
```

```
Not sure why I'm bothering, but...  How is that indexes make it harder to express a problem?

Here are some code snippets I just put in to production a few weeks ago:

 01  wire-table.                                                 
     05  wire-tag-cnt            pic 999.                        
     05  wire-tag-entry          occurs 100 times                
                                        depending on wire-tag-cnt
                                        ascending key wte-tag    
                                        indexed by tagidx.       
         10.                                                     
             15  wte-tag         pic x(4).                       
             15  wte-tag-name    pic x(50).                      
             15  wte-elem-cnt    pic 99.                         
         10                      occurs 30 times                 
                                        indexed by elemidx.      
             15  wte-element-nbr pic 99.                         
             15  wte-element-name pic x(50).                     
             15  wte-element-show-name pic x.                    
             15  wte-element-cust-show pic x.                    
             15  wte-element-code pic x(4).                      
             15  wte-type        pic x.                          

 01  value-text.                                             
     05  pic x occurs 1 to 15000 depending on value-text-len.
 77  value-text-len              pic 9(7) comp.              

 linkage section.
 77  fedwire-len                 pic s9(9) comp.          
 01  fedwire-buffer.                                      
     05  pic x occurs 1 to 20000 depending on fedwire-len.
 copy wireinfo.                                           

 procedure division using fedwire-len fedwire-buffer wireinfo.  
     ...   

 process-elements.                          
     search all wire-tag-entry              
     at end                                 
         display 'tag not found: ' wire-tag 
     when wte-tag (tagidx) = wire-tag       
         perform handle-elements            
     end-search                             
     .                                      
                                            
 handle-elements.            
     move 0 to value-text-len
     set elemidx to 1        
     perform parse-elements  
     .                       
                             
 parse-elements.                                            
     move space to delim-1                           
     set process-element to true                     
     perform parse-element                           
             until wte-type (tagidx elemidx) = spaces
                or elemidx > max-elem-per-tag        
                or delim-1 = '{'                     
                or wirebuf-pos > fedwire-len         
     .                                               
 parse-element.                                            
     move wte-len (tagidx elemidx) to value-text-len
     move space to delim-1                                        
     evaluate wte-type (tagidx elemidx)                           
     when 'F'                                                     
         unstring fedwire-buffer into value-text                  
                  pointer wirebuf-pos                             
     when 'V'                                                     
         unstring fedwire-buffer delimited by '*' or '{'          
                  into value-text delimiter in delim-1            
                  pointer wirebuf-pos                             
     when '4'                                                     
         unstring fedwire-buffer into hold-len pointer wirebuf-pos
         move hold-len to value-text-len                          
         unstring fedwire-buffer into value-text
                  pointer wirebuf-pos                             
     when other                                                   
         display 'invalid type in parse-element: '                
                 wte-type (tagidx elemidx)                        
         call fb-abend                                                 
     end-evaluate
     move wte-element-nbr(tagidx elemidx) to wire-tag-elem
     perform write-to-wireinfo                        
     set elemidx up by 1                                  
     .                                                    

  write-to-wireinfo.                                
 *    evaluate wire-tag also wire-tag-elem-nbr      
      evaluate wire-tag also elemidx                
      when '1100' also 01                           
          move value-text to wi-format-version (1:) 
      when '1100' also 02                           
          move value-text to wi-test-prod-code    
      ...
      when '9000' also any                       
          move value-text to wi-ff-line (ffidx)  
          set ffidx up by 1                      
      end-evaluate                               
      move zero to value-text-len                        
      .

Also demonstrates some usefulness of occurs depending on.

The one thing that did annoy me is that I had to initialize some indexes like this:
set ibkidx bbkidx bnfidx drwidx orgidx orgfidx obkidx insidx
    obiidx rbkiidx drwaidx ibkiidx ibkaidx bbkiidx bbkaidx  
    bnfiidx bnfaidx bbiidx ffidx                            
    to 1                                                    

Yikes!

And yes, in 2011 I am using level 77 items!

Frank
```

```
Frank Swarbrick wrote:
> Not sure why I'm bothering, but...  How is that indexes make it
> harder to express a problem?

See "Yikes!" below.

You answered your own question :-)

(Actually, I use indexes for all table reference in COBOL, but I think 
Robert has some good points.)

>
> Here are some code snippets I just put in to production a few weeks
…[112 more quoted lines elided]…
> Frank

Frank, I'm not going to comment on the code (other than to agree with 
"Yikes!"), but I did want to say it is courageous to post code here. :-)

For myself, if faced with this problem, I would choose a language that 
easily supports Regular Expressions (COBOL does so clumsily, and it is 
platform dependent), but I accept you wanted to demonstrate use of some 
constructs which have been the subject of debate here and also that COBOL is 
the tool you have.

It comes down to a personal choice in the end (or a choice imposed by the 
COI).

Despite all the discussion and debate regarding indexes and optimization, 
unless somebody implements the improved standard, it is just bubbles.

Robert observed that the last credible standard was the 85 one and I agree 
with him. Neverthless, a huge amount of work went into the 2002 COBOL 
standard and it is a pity to see it dismissed as not useful by most vendors. 
(Some vendors have implemented quite a bit of it but, as far as I know, 
nobody has implemented all of it. Meanwhile, COBOL has lost credibility and 
the world is voting with its feet.) You can argue all you like about 77 
levels, OCCURS...DEPENDING ON, indexes versus subscripts, or any other 
aspect of Ye Olde COBOL but if Ye Olde COBOL has no relevance then your 
argument is like hoar frost in sunshine. Most COBOL users are stuck with 
COBOL 85 for whatever platform they are using. That makes it hard to argue a 
case for continued use of COBOL. Why would you pay money to be tied to a 27 
year old standard that doesn't play nicely with modern platforms and 
environments (unless you implement the OO form of it, and most practitioners 
dismiss OOP as unnecessary - usually because their opinion about that is 
uninformed...) when you can simply let go of it and get something better for 
free?

Please note I have not contributed to the latter part of this thread (which 
is now becoming tiresome, at least, it is to me... I respect the right of 
the posters to continue ad infinitum... :-)) as I see no point in it.

What minds will be changed? Will COBOL be improved? Hey! the sun is 
shining... I'm off to the beach. :-)

Pete.
```

```
On Wed, 7 Dec 2011 08:48:18 -0800 (PST), Frank Swarbrick
<fswarbrick@gmail.com> wrote:

>Not sure why I'm bothering, but...  How is that indexes make it harder to express a problem?
>
…[109 more quoted lines elided]…
>And yes, in 2011 I am using level 77 items!


In terms of expressiveness, you could have done the above (and
honestly, I've not taken the time to look at it too closely), just as
easily with subscripts.  Ignoring for a moment that SEARCH (ALL)
requires the use of a subscript.  Other than changing a few SETs to
ADDs or MOVEs, your code would be unchanged.  Your code snippet also
seems to avoid any of the places where indexes are awkward.

By adding a *second* way to specify subscripts, you (1) have to decide
to use (or not) indexes, (2) use different, and more limited
statements to manipulate indexes, and (3) deal with additional
restrictions in how you can actually use an index.  And while those
are not huge items, they are necessarily more complex than just using
subscripts.  For that added complexity, you get *no* improvement in
expressiveness, just additional work.

Where I started this thread was all that then remains is a performance
improvement, and the reality of that is more than a little
questionable (hence all the talk of strength reduction in 1958 Fortran
compilers).  And even if not, in the context of 50 year old compilers,
a conceptually much simpler implementation of indexes - both from the
programmer's and compiler writer's perspective - was trivial.  And if
even that isn't there (IOW, there are essentially no benefits, yet
there are downsides), aren't indexes a mistake?
```

```
Robert Wessel wrote:

No, not a mistake; maybe a not-totally-necessary alternative.

I've folllowed your arguments, Robert, and, although I always use indexes 
for table reference in COBOL, I kept an open mind.

IF the optimization had been implemented so there was no actual need for an 
INDEX type, you might have a case. But it wasn't, so, at least in my 
opinion, you don't.

The reality is that people have a choice between subscripting and/or 
indexing, just as they have a choice about writing "add 1 to count" or 
"compute count = count + 1".

I can't see a downside to having options... :-)
```

```
On Thu, 8 Dec 2011 22:57:39 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>Robert Wessel wrote:
>> On Wed, 7 Dec 2011 08:48:18 -0800 (PST), Frank Swarbrick
…[156 more quoted lines elided]…
>I can't see a downside to having options... :-)


Only if they provide real choices, or present at some real advantages.
"Add 1 to count" (or the C "+=" operator, for example), provides a
rather more concise, and clearer, way of altering a variable in a
simple way - certainly clearer than the compute - and shorter and less
error prone to boot.  I don't, however, expect different code to be
generated for the two statements.  OTOH, a complex expression is *not*
going to be clearer if expressed as a long sequence of ADDs,
MULTIPLYs, etc., instead of a COMPUTE.  But "SET index UP BY 1"
appears to offer no advantages over an ADD.

But the question remains - why was the optimization not implemented? I
think we've established that contemporaneous compilers for other
languages did do optimizations similar to that.  "Cobol is special"
might well be the actual reason, but it feels a bit thin.

And if an optimization hint was necessary for the compiler technology
of the era, then why not simply let us treat it is a numeric value as
it basically is, and allow us to write "ADD 1 TO index" as well?  Or
"COMPUTE index2 = index1 + 3"?  After all, you've always been allowed
to write "IF index > numeric-field", so it's not like the compiler was
ever unable to do the conversion from index to subscript automatically
anyway.   At which point most of my objection goes away.

So (1) I'm not convinced there was an actual performance reason to do
indexes, and (2) if there was, they were implemented badly.

Stubborn cuss, aren't I?
```

```
On Dec 8, 11:35 am, Frank Swarbrick <fswarbr...@gmail.com> wrote:
> What are others?  The only other one I've found is I cannot "DISPLAY" them (DISPLAY MYIDX) during debugging.

I can't know what you may have tried, but index values may
be displayed indirectly. YMMV
=====
       identification division.
       program-id. idx-dbg.
       data division.
       working-storage section.
       77 dbg-index index.
       77 dbg-comp redefines dbg-index comp pic 9(8).
       1 a-table.
        2 occurs 100000 indexed by te-idx.
         3 table-entry pic x(40).
       procedure division.
       begin.
           set te-idx to 1
           set dbg-index to te-idx
           display dbg-comp
           set te-idx to 100000
           set dbg-index to te-idx
           display dbg-comp
           set te-idx to 50001
           set dbg-index to te-idx
           display dbg-comp
           stop run
           .
=====
00000000
03999960
02000000
```

```
While I've decided to stop discussing this with you, I do want to
agree that I misremembered my quote.  It was not Hopper, rather Jean
Sammet, another member of the CODASYL committee which developed Cobol,
who said:

"The users for who COBOL was designed were actually two subclasses of
those people concerned with business data processing problems. One is
the relatively inexperienced programmer for whom the naturalness of
COBOL would be an asset, while the other type of user would be
essentially anybody who had not written the program initially. In
other words, the readability of COBOL programs would provide
documentation to all who might wish to examine the programs, including
the supervisory or management personnel. Little attempt was made to
cater for professional programmers."
```

```
On Dec 8, 8:06 pm, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
> On Wed, 7 Dec 2011 10:10:19 -0800 (PST), Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz>
> wrote:
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> While I've decided to stop discussing this with you,

That is a pity because I was hoping that you would eventually get
around to attempting to substantiate your assertion that COBOL CALLs
were not "proper" and were "unusable". But I wasn't holding my breath.


> I do want to
> agree that I misremembered my quote.  It was not Hopper, rather Jean
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> cater for professional programmers."

You seem to misunderstand the context of that comment. In the 1950s,
when COBOL was being designed, programmers were expected to rewire
computers, structure their programs around the timings of mercury tube
memory, be able code directly in binary, and debug the hardware.

Those are the people who were referred to in the 1950s as
'professional programmers'. They were not interested in mere business
applications (except the LEO team of course). A language designed for
them would turn out to be like ACL (Atlas Commercial Language) or BCPL
(Basic Combined Programming Language). The COBOL designers were not
interested in converting those into high-level language programmers.

COBOL certainly was intended for people who had experience in business
(rather than electronics) and knew a packing slip from a credit note,
as well as 'inexperienced' (ie third generation) programmers. In the
60s these became professional programmers too, just on different types
of applications.

As counter examples BASIC and Algol-68W (which became Pascal) were
intended purely as teaching languages and would not be used
professionally.

There is (or was in the late 1950s) a distinction between
'professional programmers' and those who programmed professionally (as
distinct from amateurs such as civil engineers writing FORTRAN).


> >> >Dealing with currency is not a CS problem, it _is_ a 'professional
> >> >programmer' problem.
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> Cobol long ago?

They certainly moved on from complaining about what happened in the
60s. You haven't though.
```

```
On Dec 7, 8:18 am, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:

You were advocating that inline code aided clarity, or specifically
that taking blocks out-of-line was a "bad idea in terms of clarity".

The ultimate expression of this is the program that is entirely in-
line, which is thus entirely monolithic.

If you learned programming in FORTRAN then you may well think that in-
line is 'better' because that is the only way to do it. COBOL
programmers may well think that out-of line is perfectly clear given
adequate paragraph naming.

It may well be that a high level structure as:

          PERFORM Open-Product-File
          PERFORM Process-Product-Records
              UNTIL End-Of-Product-File
                 OR Fatal-Error
          PERFORM Close-Product-File

is perfectly clear as to what is intended while putting all the
processing code in-line (and thus without the paragraph names) would
be considerably less clear.

'Clarity' is entirely what one is used to.
```

```
On Dec 5, 11:37 pm, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
> On Mon, 5 Dec 2011 19:06:25 -0800 (PST), Rick Smith
> <rs847...@gmail.com> wrote:

[snip]

> >Though I could not find references to the early '60s, I am
> >willing to accept that indexing ("strength reduction" for
…[28 more quoted lines elided]…
> been a far better use of everybody's time than indexes.

What is now considered "proper" came wih structured programming,
in the early '70s. Indexing was circa 1965.

> My complaint about Cobol indexes is less the INDEXED BY clause, and
> more the absurdly separate method of manipulating them.

[Data-names as] subscripts belong to class numeric. [Index-names
as] subscripts belong to class index. Different classes. Different
operators. Quite reasonable!

> If you want to provide an optimization hint (I'd take a skeptical
> stance about the hint being useful in the long term*, although in the
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> subscript).

This gets back to the "size of a table entry" issue. Unless the size
of entries in the two tables is the same, stepping through the tables
will occur at diffent rates. Some compilers have pemitted the same
index-name to be used with two different table, when the size of
entries is the same; it is not permitted by the standard.

>  OTOH, reversing it makes some sense too (IOW, allow a
> numeric field to say "INDEXES TABLE-1").

> But it should at least be an ordinary numeric variable for all other
> purposes.

This is a conflict between numeric and index classes and their
distinct limitations. Consider that 'move 0 to numeric-field'
is invalid as class index, but valid as class numeric. While
'set index-name to 0' IS a syntax error. Your suggestion means
that at some later point the compiler will have to note that
the 'move 0 ...' WAS a syntax error.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Subscript and other optimizations was Re: 77 Levels

- **From:** Robert Wessel <robertwessel2@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 2011-12-06T12:37:51-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<tjlsd7t4tng6vhqq50av161s7msq0e41rl@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<ad57c1af-5f8b-4878-b812-7626224d3933@s6g2000vbc.googlegroups.com> <a5p8d7h2q63rp1epto5eq3f3r2efl6hg32@4ax.com> <79aod71i9crc4pdhspk457rodgru4hos82@4ax.com> <5fcd3cc4-c923-4d66-a6f8-828496d91fd0@q30g2000yqj.googlegroups.com> <f5dqd7hjm40r8u6g5rsbuj3a36v42s4m2d@4ax.com> <10fcbf3f-77ad-48c9-8074-5da0e2ff077b@t16g2000vba.googlegroups.com> <9c5rd7h5cdfulhophs54gk696obba9irap@4ax.com> <bf611ba3-2e72-4e8c-b1fd-b8ecd22697a0@v11g2000yqi.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Tue, 6 Dec 2011 08:43:31 -0800 (PST), Rick Smith
<rs847925@gmail.com> wrote:

>On Dec 5, 11:37ï¿½pm, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
>> On Mon, 5 Dec 2011 19:06:25 -0800 (PST), Rick Smith
…[37 more quoted lines elided]…
>in the early '70s. Indexing was circa 1965.


Not really.  By "proper," I mainly meant the loop inline with the
control structure.  Fortran's labeled loop end statement was ugly as
all heck, but still provided* that.  But if you want a more
contemporaneous example, see Algol 58, which largely introduced the
begin..end style block structuring (although hints of that date back
further).  Statement-scoped blocks, analogous to Cobol
PERFORM..END-PERFORM, date back to about the same time, even BASIC,
with FOR..NEXT, dates to 1964.


*Exclusively.  The Cobol style out-of-line loop is near unique.  I
don't particularly object to having looping on the subroutine call
statement, although it's a bit odd, but not having an inline loop
certainly adds to the difficulties of understanding a program, since
the loop controls and the loop body are now separated.  One could
argue that the languages that support only recursion, and not
iteration, cause somewhat similar pains.  Certainly *I* don't find
recursion a natural replacement for many simple loops.


>> My complaint about Cobol indexes is less the INDEXED BY clause, and
>> more the absurdly separate method of manipulating them.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>operators. Quite reasonable!


See below.


>> If you want to provide an optimization hint (I'd take a skeptical
>> stance about the hint being useful in the long term*, although in the
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>entries is the same; it is not permitted by the standard.


Which was basically my point - the hint doesn't help the two-table use
case, the compiler would have to generate the required (ugly) code to
convert the value if you used the index with the "wrong" table.  You
could use the hint to bind the index to a specific table, which gets
you the error message, or at least the compiler could issue a warning
if you really wanted.  But this is no different than what was
generated for "SET table-1-index TO table-2-index" (at this point I
don't remember if that form was originally allowed, but you could
certainly use a temporary and two SETs).

The point of making it a hint, or some-such, is that the compiler is
mostly free to ignore any such thing, if it thinks it can generate
better code.


>> ï¿½OTOH, reversing it makes some sense too (IOW, allow a
>> numeric field to say "INDEXES TABLE-1").
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>the 'move 0 ...' WAS a syntax error.


Certainly you wouldn't suggest a different set of arithmetic
statements for numeric fields with and without signs, would you? After
all "MOVE -1 to PIC-S999-FIELD" is valid while "MOVE -1 to
PIC-999-FIELD" is not.

Nor does the separation help with:

  MOVE ZERO TO subscript;
  SET index TO subscript.

And if the compiler can generate a runtime* check for that, it can
certainly do it for an arithmetic statement (and that's more-or-less
what happens to support "standard" truncation on binary fields).


*Or a compile time check, for cases involving expressions that are
effectively compile time constants, and sufficiently cleaver
compilers, although there are clear limits to that you can do at
compile time, even assuming infinite cleverness on the part of the
compiler.
```

```
On Dec 6, 1:37 pm, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
> On Tue, 6 Dec 2011 08:43:31 -0800 (PST), Rick Smith
> <rs847...@gmail.com> wrote:
> >On Dec 5, 11:37 pm, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:

[snip]

> >> Although I'd argue that adding a proper looping construct would have
> >> been a far better use of everybody's time than indexes.
…[29 more quoted lines elided]…
> See below.

[snip]

> >> OTOH, reversing it makes some sense too (IOW, allow a
> >> numeric field to say "INDEXES TABLE-1").
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> PIC-999-FIELD" is not.

With all due respect, your question is a non sequitur; numeric
fields are class numeric and there are specific rules that
apply to data items of that class. One of those is that moving
a negative value to an unsigned field causes the sign to be
dropped. "MOVE -1 to PIC-999-FIELD" is a valid move and the
result is, likely, "001".

> Nor does the separation help with:
>
>   MOVE ZERO TO subscript;
>   SET index TO subscript.

While 'technically' and seemingly invalid, the above is acceptable
because evaluation of 'index' is delayed until it is actually used
to reference a table element.

Clarification: While 'SET index-name TO 0' was invalid in COBOL 85,
the 2002 standard has relaxed the rules somewhat.

ISO/IEC 1989:2002(E) Substantive changes not affecting existing
programs
"121) SET index-name. The result of setting an index can be outside
      of the range of the associated table. Also, the setting of an
      index in the TO phrase does not have to be within the range
      of the associated table."
```

```
On Tue, 6 Dec 2011 13:29:27 -0800 (PST), Rick Smith
<rs847925@gmail.com> wrote:

>On Dec 6, 1:37ï¿½pm, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
>> On Tue, 6 Dec 2011 08:43:31 -0800 (PST), Rick Smith
…[63 more quoted lines elided]…
>result is, likely, "001".


And the extension of the ordinary numeric rules to a natural* type
with a restricted range is pretty obvious and trivial. And its
internal representation is of rather limited interest.

And note that Cobol *does* allow you to interchange indexes and
ordinary numeric items in a bunch of places.  Obviously in the
subscripts after an array reference, the VARYING clause of a PERFORM
(IOW, there's no separate "VARYING-INDEX" clause if you wanted to loop
while modifying an index), the IF statement and UNTIL clauses.  IOW,
you *can* write:

 MOVE 7 TO numeric-field
 SET index to 5
 IF index > numeric-field ...

And that *will* evaluate as "IF 5 > 7", as you'd expect.

So why not the other places?


*Natural number in the traditional sense
```

- **References:** `<79aod71i9crc4pdhspk457rodgru4hos82@4ax.com> <5fcd3cc4-c923-4d66-a6f8-828496d91fd0@q30g2000yqj.googlegroups.com> <f5dqd7hjm40r8u6g5rsbuj3a36v42s4m2d@4ax.com> <10fcbf3f-77ad-48c9-8074-5da0e2ff077b@t16g2000vba.googlegroups.com> <9c5rd7h5cdfulhophs54gk696obba9irap@4ax.com> <bf611ba3-2e72-4e8c-b1fd-b8ecd22697a0@v11g2000yqi.googlegroups.com> <tjlsd7t4tng6vhqq50av161s7msq0e41rl@4ax.com> <a4fce042-2895-474d-914d-6844be441cff@4g2000yqu.googlegroups.com> <ve4ud7l9g6j607r0bdj851epbipk17khci@4ax.com>`

```
On Dec 7, 2:45 am, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
> On Tue, 6 Dec 2011 13:29:27 -0800 (PST), Rick Smith
> <rs847...@gmail.com> wrote:
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
[snip]
>
> >> >> OTOH, reversing it makes some sense too (IOW, allow a
…[39 more quoted lines elided]…
> And that *will* evaluate as "IF 5 > 7", as you'd expect.

Here is the rule (2002):
"Relation tests may be made only between ...
2) an index-name and a numeric data item or numeric
   literal. The occurrence number that corresponds to
   the value of the index-name is compared to the data
   item or literal."

IOW, the comparison will be made _as if_ the '7' were
an occurrence number. The implementor is free to use any
means that will achieve the correct result.

Given that the length of one entry in the table associated
with 'index' is 16 bytes. The comparison _could_ be:
    if 64 > 96
which is different than you think one should expect!

> So why not the other places?

While occurrence numbers belong to a limited set of natural
numbers: n = {1, 2, 3, ..., m-2, m-1, m}, indexes are not
required to belong to that set. Given a 16-byte 'stride'
the set of index values is: i = {0, 16, 32, ..., (m-3)*16,
(m-2)*16, (m-1)*16}.

> *Natural number in the traditional sense
```

```
PERFORM is _not_ a "subroutine call". Perhaps this is where your

from.

PERFORM is an out-of-line block execute. CALL is COBOL's "subroutine
call" and has been since the 60s.

Early BASIC had GOSUB which is roughly what COBOL's PERFORM does.

together.

> convert the value if you used the index with the "wrong" table.  You
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
```

```

"Robert Wessel" <robertwessel2@yahoo.com> wrote in message 
news:a5p8d7h2q63rp1epto5eq3f3r2efl6hg32@4ax.com...
> On Wed, 23 Nov 2011 16:59:00 -0800 (PST), Richard
> <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>
<snip>
>>COBOL can use dynamic CALLs or it can use static linked CALLs. Nested
>>program CALLs may well be less expensive than external CALLs but these
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>
 <replying to an old note>

Check out the IBM (mainframe) COBOL performance paper.  CALLs to nested 
programs perform SIGNFICANTLY better than CALL (static or dynamic) to 
external programs.

With Micro Focus, the CALL to a nested program produces code MUCH more 
similar to PERFORM than to CALL (external program).

In fact, I am unaware of ANY COBOL implementation that actually uses the 
same (internal) mechanism for calls to nested and external programs.
```

```
On Wed, 23 Nov 2011 14:50:34 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"

>SkippyPB wrote:
>> On Tue, 22 Nov 2011 02:48:04 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
>make it comp, which IS valid in IBM COBOL.

COMP-5 is implementer defined and on the IBM z series is used for
Binary items where the value can exceed the size of the picture but
not the implied size of the binary work such as 
"77 BIN32767 PIC S9(4) COMP-5 VALUE 32767.".  It is not little-endian.
See https://www-304.ibm.com/support/docview.wss?uid=swg27001516&aid=1
for more information.  Note that this is from a 2004 presentation.

Clark Morris

>> rest snipped
>
```

```
"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:8260d0aa-ecdc-4abe-8efc-2540e0fbbcea@h42g2000yqd.googlegroups.com...
On Nov 24, 8:52 am, Rick Smith <rs847...@gmail.com> wrote:
> On Nov 22, 8:28 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
>
<snip>
>  However, in
> terms of the COBOL language, "subscript" is an index-name
> used as a subscript and "index" is a data-name used as a
> subscript.
>
I doubt that anyone cares and this is a reply to an "old" note, but the 
maning of "subscripting" in COBOL has changed from Standard to Standard.  It 
(since the '85 Standard) includes the use of an index-name  (an index 
data-item may NOT be used as a subscript - or as an index in the old terms).

NOTE: the noun  "subscript" (as opposed ot "subscripting") does NOT include 
index names (past or present COBOL Standards).

As I say, this is relatively obscure trivia, but does help explain some 
rules in the current (and '85) Standards that use the term "subscripting" 
(or subscript as a verb) and include the use of index names.  The further 
confusion with index data items (i.e. USAGE INDEX) makes all of ths hard to 
read, but (I think) actually consistent in the Standard itself.
```

- **References:** `<vo5pq.3$1x3.0@en-nntp-16.dc1.easynews.com> <a35c4935-d3b0-4730-9c2c-cbc823e9774d@h30g2000pro.googlegroups.com> <x7KdnehXMqqpXzXTnZ2dnUVZ_qCdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <7e8baa4c-b383-4b0b-9ea7-9cfe7d513c5d@q39g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <9ZidnXpLONzbRDTTnZ2dnUVZ_hWdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <7d20edb2-dc60-438b-b099-169ac73ddc3e@o13g2000vbo.googlegroups.com> <o0qlc7ptg77js09mciqesjs7ooee7odc7q@4ax.com> <9j0c8sFsj7U1@mid.individual.net> <oe6mc7pappl2bggr3cjpb8jbd46nu93qvt@4ax.com> <9j1b0qFcqmU1@mid.individual.net> <ab4750c4-a540-4b6b-aabe-8e784bec03c3@t16g2000vba.googlegroups.com> <9j30haFf5mU1@mid.individual.net> <3bc440eb-d629-44bf-bcb8-2c8dced6dc37@r9g2000vbw.googlegroups.com> <8260d0aa-ecdc-4abe-8efc-2540e0fbbcea@h42g2000yqd.googlegroups.com> <1TJIq.237122$mV1.127705@en-nntp-04.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
On Dec 22, 12:34 pm, "Bill Klein" <wmkl...@noreply.ix.netcom.com>
wrote:

[attributions corrected]

> On Nov 24, 8:52 am, Rick Smith <rs847...@gmail.com> wrote:
>
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> index names (past or present COBOL Standards).

Technically (ISO/IEC 1989:2002, 8.4.1.2.3 GR 1), the noun "subscript"
does not include data-name, either! Though in (ISO/IEC 1989:2002,
8.4.1.2.1) either may be _used as_ a subscript. <g>

> As I say, this is relatively obscure trivia, but does help explain some
> rules in the current (and '85) Standards that use the term "subscripting"
> (or subscript as a verb) and include the use of index names.  The further
> confusion with index data items (i.e. USAGE INDEX) makes all of ths hard to
> read, but (I think) actually consistent in the Standard itself.

The obscurity arises from what is fundamental to both "subscripting"
and "indexing", that a subscript is an occurence number. The standard
then provides different means to arrive at an occurence number.

The rules for subscript seem consistent with one of stated goals
of The COBOL Standard Style Guide:

"48. The normative portion of the specification is a precise
     specification and is not targeted for the user or for the
     implementor. Therefore, some specifications might be somewhat
     difficult for a casual reader to understand. The informative
     portion of the specification is intended to be understood by
     a user and an implementor and needs not be so precise."
```

```
On 11/21/2011 5:13 PM, john@wexfordpress.com wrote:
> On Oct 27, 5:20 pm, "HeyBub"<hey...@NOSPAMgmail.com>  wrote:
>> Richard wrote:
…[37 more quoted lines elided]…
> John Culleton


I agree completely with the readability point.

When I first encountered COBOL (circa 1968 IIRC), the first thing I 
asked my colleague was whether or not one could define fields within a 
paragraph (or a section I suppose) that were somewhat "local" to that 
code. Of course, the way COBOL compilers allocate WORKING-STORAGE, such 
fields would likely have to be available globally, but I would love to 
have a choice to be able to include "LOCAL" as a keyword to force the 
item to be initialized on entry to the paragraph if I found that handy...

The benefit of in-line definitions would be to make it very easy to see 
at a glance the definition of work fields etc. rather than flipping 
through several hundred pages of WS listings (ok, I know we all use 
editors and search commands, but allow me the trip down memory lane).

8000-FORMAT-PRICE.

     05  prt-price pic zzz,zzz,zz9.99- .

     compute ...
```
