```
This is exactly what happens with object methods in OO COBOL.

You can use the Factory to maintain global data across all instances of an 
object , the object storage to maintain data across all methods of an 
instance, and the local working storage is availble to each method. Fujitsu 
implement it by use of nested COBOL programs and you could do that too if 
you don't want to get into OOP.

Pete.
```

```
He didn't say "computer science degree" he said "computer science
background".  There is a major difference.  Our department has a
MS program in Software Engineering.  I frequently argue with them
that we did a lot more actual "engineering" before this name was
even coined.  I didn't get any kind of degree until I had already
been doing this computer stuff for over 30 years.  I read a lot. I
played on computers a lot (and I don't mean Angry Birds).  I like
to think that long before they started passing out pieces of paper
that said BS-CS on them we had very strong "backgrounds" in "computer
science".

I agree totally with the idea posited by the article.  There are way
too many people out there claiming to be computer professionals who
have right to make that claim.


bill
```

```
My experience as a manager of COBOL programmers from 1968 to 1984 was
that Computer Science departments existed to teach
students to hate COBOL. The professors never used COBOL and were fans
of FORTRAN and other engineering-oriented languages.

I never took a college course in Computer Science. I learned the
language from the IBM Green Books, from the publications
of Mike Murach and Associates, from peeking over the shoulder of other
programmers, and from RTFM.

I still think in COBOL 1968. So sue me.
```

```
In article <051c775c-dd9c-43f8-941d-827d0ce29882@h11g2000yqd.googlegroups.com>,
	"john@wexfordpress.com" <john@wexfordpress.com> writes:
> On Dec 12, 2:55ï¿½pm, billg...@cs.uofs.edu (Bill Gunshannon) wrote:
>> In article <dd7c6a73-48be-431d-81df-85b30f853...@t16g2000vba.googlegroups.com>,
…[46 more quoted lines elided]…
> students to hate COBOL. 

Well, after 23 years in academia, I would have to disagree.  They taught
COBOL here (and taught it well) and at a number of other Universities I
am familiar with.

>                         The professors never used COBOL 

Well, that's another problem entirely, depending on your definition of
"used".  My biggest gripe with academia is the fact that most professors
went straight from being students to being professors and have never
"used" any programming language.

>                                                         and were fans
> of FORTRAN and other engineering-oriented languages.

I find that easch professor has his favorite language.  You can pretty
much figure out which one by looking at what was in vogue in academia
when they were an undergrad.  It's a lot like mother duck syndrome.  The
only expceptions I see are the professors who have no favorite but prefer
to jump on whatever bandwagon is currently passing by.

> I never took a college course in Computer Science. 

I went to the Army Programmer/Analyst School in Indiana and the course
was patterned after a college course as they were hoping to get credit
for the people who took it.  They did eventually but not til long after
it made any difference to me.  It was a 16 week course.  I did all of
the work in about 4 weeks but was required to stay the whole 16 weeks
so they filled in my time by having me do the Fortran, IBM-BASIC, and
360 Assembler courses.  And after I left I never worked on an IBM Main-
frame again.  :-)

>                                                    I learned the
> language from the IBM Green Books, from the publications
> of Mike Murach and Associates, from peeking over the shoulder of other
> programmers, and from RTFM.

I was also self-taught.  the same for the several dozen other languages
I learned as the need arose. This just seemed to be the work I had a
natural ability for.

> I still think in COBOL 1968. So sue me.

Well, I let myself drift forward to 85 but that's where I stop.  if
they sue you I am sure there are enough others that it will have to
a class action suit.  :-)

bill
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Java apps have most flaws, Cobol apps the least

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-12-15T02:35:16+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9krn0lF8kbU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<slrnje6uah.c31.rodo@seeteufel.bloerp.de> <880bb748-460f-4446-937e-3f32e8a55a2b@d10g2000vbf.googlegroups.com> <dd7c6a73-48be-431d-81df-85b30f8534b0@t16g2000vba.googlegroups.com> <9kn4h2Fc2dU1@mid.individual.net> <73fabb59-fc82-4c32-bf4b-323be45279cf@q11g2000vbq.googlegroups.com>`

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Dec 12, 7:55 pm, billg...@cs.uofs.edu (Bill Gunshannon) wrote:
>> In article
…[41 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Hmmm.... Over a long career (more than 4 decades) working around the planet 
I have worked with people who have a comp. sci. background and with people 
who don't.

I have met outstanding people in both categories, so I'm not sure that it 
really matters. Particularly not for COBOL. I tend to judge things by 
results (although most of the time I try not be judgemental at all). I would 
agree that you don't need a comp. sci. qualification to be a COBOL 
programmer, but I don't think it would do any harm. I started out working on 
commercial applications then moved to a company which focused on scientific 
computing and became involved in system software. I made a point of getting 
as much wide and varied experience as I could on different platforms and 
application environments, because it was always my plan to become an 
independent consultant. I have never regretted my choice of career nor the 
decisions I took within that career. Although I followed a growth path which 
took me out of programming for a living, I have always loved it and will 
probably be writing software as long as I can see and type. I still get a 
kick out of watching code run correctly (and I still fall apart when it 
doesn't... :-)) I am in a decline at the moment because I have been trying 
for some days now to get something working which really should be simple and 
it isn't there yet. Sure, I have learned much in the past week, but I still 
don't have a result :-). I'll be suicidal by the weekend if I don't get it 
solved... :-)

I believe that people can worry far too much about their qualifications on 
paper. In my experience, a track record trumps a PhD. (I was contending for 
a particular job against a guy who had one and I got it. They gave him 
another position in IT in the same company and he proved to be a disaster 
when it came to the coal face and actually getting a result. Eventually he 
left in disgrace... So, then people get into arguments about whether a PhD 
from Yale is better than a PhD from Harvard, or Oxford or Cambridge and so 
on... It really doesn't matter. What matters are results.

Almost everything I know about IT I learned on the job and in my spare time, 
teaching myself. It is an ongoing process and I'm still learning. I don't 
believe a comp. sci. background is worthless because I don't really believe 
that ANY formal education is worthless. At the very least, it imposes a 
discipline and teaches young people to enquire and dig for information which 
is pretty important as part of long term career and personal growth. But my 
advice would be not to get obsessed with the paper chase. Start producing 
results and get known for that. It won't matter if you don't have a PhD.

I have had a few occasions in my career where people have asked if I have a 
PhD. My usual response is:"No, but I can buy one if it's important to 
you..." :-)



>>
>> I agree totally with the idea posited by the article. There are way
>> too many people out there claiming to be computer professionals who
>> have right to make that claim.

"Computer professional" eh?  Anyone who makes a living from providing 
computer based goods and services would have a perfect right to call 
themselves that. It's just pompous and silly to decide that if you're not a 
programmer you can't be a "professional". I know people who make a living 
out of testing other people's software. They are every bit as serious and 
professional in their job as the person who wrote the software they are 
testing.

Just like with certificates, people can get too obsessed over titles. If you 
make a living at it, apply ethics and integrity in your daily dealings, act 
honestly and responsibly, who's to say you are NOT a professional?

When I was working as a contract programmer I liked it when people asked me 
what my job title was. I invariably replied: "Cyberwhore."  It was something 
I'm good at, something I enjoy, and I did it for money. Not so far away from 
the ladies of the night if you are honest...

The bottom line is: Don't be obsessed by titles and bits of paper and 
whether you are a Member of the Professional Society... It is all bollocks 
if you can't produce results.

And don't get too pompous about what you do. I was at a party back in the 
60s where a guy was sucking on a pipe trying to impress some girls. I 
cringed when I heard him say: "Well, speaking as a computer programmer..."

Another guy standing nearby jumped in immediately and said:"Computer 
programmer?!!! That's just a Space Age clerk..!" The computer guy slunk away 
completely destroyed. Abut that time somebody asked me what I did.

"I'm a postman...what do you do?"

Pete.
```

```
Would you please trim your quotes thank you very much.

Now...

> > honestly and responsibly, who's to say you are NOT a professional?

Professions are prostitutes, lawyers (the 2nd oldest and not clear how
distinguished from the first nor from used car salesmen) and doctors. Any
other jobs weren't traditionally considered professions. Now that you can
get a Professional Engineer certification in certain engineering
disciplines, I suppose you can add that to the list.

> > When I was working as a contract programmer I liked it when people asked
> > me what my job title was. I invariably replied: "Cyberwhore."  It was
> > something I'm good at, something I enjoy, and I did it for money. Not so
> > far away from the ladies of the night if you are honest...

And fits in perfectly with the historically accepted list shown above.

> I recently had to put my "Profession" on a form I was filling in for a 
> government clerk... I unashamedly put down "computer programmer" and she 
> looked at it and said (more or less) "is that a profession?" with quite 
> a pompous air... 

You should have asked what she would put down for herself if filling out the
same form. Perhaps being a clerk was only her day job and indeed she was a
professional after all, during the late evening and weekend hours. Never
hurts to ask.

Computer programming is not a profession in the classical sense, but then
unless you're a prostitute, lawyer or doctor or perhaps license P.E. then
the form isn't very useful, is it?
```

```
didn't have a concurrent effect of increasing the bottom line, I would

skirt

prostitute."

exactly what he said. He breached a gentleman's agreement with me at
the request of a broker. He saw nothing wrong with that. If somebody
pays you to break your word, that was ample justification for him.

It's not for me. (Refer back to my definition of professional, above.)
```

```
Ken wrote:
> On Dec 14, 8:35 am, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

This is an excellent and interesting response, Ken. It has given me an 
excuse to take a break and respond to it. :-)

>> Hmmm.... Over a long career (more than 4 decades) working around the
>> planet I have worked with people who have a comp. sci. background
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> it yourself. (Read on.)

Perhaps we need to be a bit careful about what actually matters in our 
lives.

"Thus is the native hue of resolution sicklied o'er with the pale cast of 
thought. And enterprises of great pitch and moment, with this regard their 
currents turn awry, and lose the name of action." (quoted from memory, may 
not be exactly accurate)

I guess I'm agreeing with Shakespeare that you can overthink significance 
into things and forget all about actually doing them. For me at least it is 
important to stay focussed on getting the results and the rest of it is 
secondary.

I should also stress that your right to disagree and hold a different 
opinion does actually matter to me... :-)
>
>> I tend to judge things by
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> that are language and tool independent.

Well, that is certainly one definition. But it isn't the only one and my 
point was that people (especially young people embarking on a career) can be 
a bit too pressured into seeing the tick in the box or the piece of paper at 
the end of the course as being more important than actually developing 
competence. It is probably because having a piece of paper that says you did 
comp.sci. for three years allays peoples anxieties as to whether you are 
competent, by ANY definition of what comp. sci. is.

"Hey, I dunno what comp.sci. actually IS, but this guys done three years of 
it so he is probably OK... " Then we ask him to take a look at the invoicing 
program and the poor bugger is totally lost. He isn't incompetent, but we 
allowed a piece of paper to overrule a proper recruitment process. I used to 
take samples of my work to interviews in the early days and I usually got 
the jobs I was after. I also asked people to bring samples of their work to 
interviews when I was recruiting.

The results are what matters.

>
> <<snip>>
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> hazard.

Personally, I believe he had a Mickey Mouse Doctorate in Hamburgerology. I 
remember asking him what his thesis had been on over coffee in the cafeteria 
one day and he changed the subject very quickly. Another PhD I once worked 
with (who I liked a lot; he was a character) had received his Doctorate as 
part payment for an administration system he had put in on a College Campus 
in the USA. It took two years and there had been several previous failed 
attempts. He was Welsh and so he saw a chance to get something worth more 
than money to him (a Doctorate cuts a lot of ice back in the green hills and 
valleys...) and an honorary degree is still a degree...

Having quoted a few shabby examples I must hasten to add that there are 
dedicated men and women who ARE striving to "increase Man's knowledge of 
things" and they deserve our respect. I'm not anti-learning or 
anti-qualification; I am anti-over-emphasizing it.

I have a Yacht Master's Certificate, a Private Pilot's Licence, and I can 
drive a car and ride a motorcycle (I have the paperwork to prove it...) Does 
it make me a better programmer?  Probably not, but the action of acquiring 
these skills may have a crossover into other areas, and it certainly shows I 
can apply myself to things.

It is easy to think that the academic pursuit of something automatically 
ensures capability at it. For example, if I took you for a flight and you 
were scared to death, does the fact I have a licence make it any better? 
However, If I DIDN'T have a licence but got us down in one piece and we all 
walked away from the landing (the definition of a "good landing"), would the 
fact that I didn't have a licence make any difference?

The results are what matters.

>
>>
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> coders.

What about contractors? I wrote code and left many sites. I still think of 
myself as professional. (I have always guaranteed the work I do and will fix 
it if it is found to be broken. No limitations... for life. (mine or the 
program's, whichever comes up first :-) It is called "responsibility".)

> Much as what Truman Capote said derisively about Jack Kerouac's work:
> "That's not writing - that's just typing!"
>

I never knew until recently that the Musical of "Guys and Dolls" is based on 
a short story by Capote. I came across it in a volume of great short 
stories, started reading it (it is delightful) and thought: "Hmmm... this 
sounds familiar..." Then the penny dropped :-)

> <<snip>>
>
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> being a professional meant to me.

Ah, yes, Ken. What it means to YOU. But there are other definitions and one 
of them is about making a living and this being described as a "profession".

Here it is: A professional is one
> who strives for, and achieves, a harmonious balance among his client's
> interest, the interests of his peers, and his own self-interest.

Not bad, but it misses some essential elements.  Responsibility, 
remuneration, heart and soul, honesty, integrity, honour... I could go on.

I once heard a "professional" described as a man "who you can close a deal 
with a handshake with, and know you won't be shafted."

There are many definitions and taking any particular one from any particular 
authority is no more valid than making up your own definition.

Hence I come back to results.
>
>> It's just pompous and silly to decide that if you're not a
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Good. So there are some elements there we can agree on. Note that they are 
NOT dependent on having a diploma...

>>
>> When I was working as a contract programmer I liked it when people
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> better than that.

No, honestly, I'm not... :-)

I don't apologise for the fact that spent around 20 years programming 
computers for money. I never ripped anybody off and I strove to ensure they 
were smiling when I finished, just like any good whore... :-)


CLC may understand you are only joking - others may
> not.
> They will think you will compromise any principle, break any rule,
> skirt
> any legal regulation, all in the pursuit of money.

But even whores don't generally do that. (I have known a few as friends not 
necessarily as a customer...:-) )

See, I don't really mind what "others" think; I don't actually mind what CLC 
thinks. I will defend to the death their right to think whatever they want 
to. (Even if I vehemently disagree with them...:-))

Anyone who has ever known or worked with me knows that I would NOT 
"compromise any principle, break any rule, skirt any legal regulation, all 
in the pursuit of money."  Anyone who hasn't had that pleasure will read my 
posts at the mercy of their own prejudices and proclivities, and there's 
nothing I can do about that. So I really have nothing to prove. :-) It isn't 
that I don't care, it is just that it isn't really important.  People who 
read these archives long after I am gone will draw their own conclusions.



I learned this the
> hard way.
> I had a "professional" peer who used to joke about being a "data
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Did the same guy ever refer to himself as "professional"? You would be 
within your rights to question his use of that appellation.

Don't you think he would have been the same in whatever field of endeavour 
he was employed?

"Professionalism" does not necessarily imply "Saintliness"... The most 
prefessional practitioners in any field will have occasional lapses.

> It's not for me. (Refer back to my definition of professional, above.)

No, not for me either. But I think we have moved out of the workplace into 
the field of Human behaviour and that is a whole different ball game :-)

Pete.
```

```
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
>Isn't that true of anything that depends on a runtime?


It depends.

Some runtime dependencies are almost ignorable - consider the
appropriate .NET framework which was probably already automatically
pushed to your user's PCs by MS (and their IT department).  And if it
hasn't been, you're almost certainly not going to get much pushback
from IT to get it installed.

Others you can install as part of your installation procedure.  Again,
not too bad a problem, just a bit of extra work for you.

Then there are those that require the user to do something to get
installed.  In some cases, the Flash Player, Acrobat reader or media
players, for example, they probably will have done some of that
already, and it's not that big a deal even if they need to do it.  But
it still usually requires an extra cycle of interaction with IT.  In
other cases, particularly with more offbeat stuff, it can be more
complex, because it's that much tougher to get IT on board.

And finally you have cases where you not only need the user to install
something, they'll need to pay for it (or you'll need to pay for it,
which can be a paperwork nightmare).  Obviously some things, like MS's
SQL Server or Host Integration Server, are things your product might
need, and that would probably be clear to the user from the start.
Although needing those will still slow things down, but at least it
likely to happen on only a limited number of machines.  But some of
the Cobol compiler runtimes are also not free, and need to be
installed on each client.  Most ISVs will run screaming from that kind
of requirement, when simple and free options abound.
```

```
Fritz Wuehler wrote:
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> Isn't that true of anything that depends on a runtime?

Yes, but some runtimes are more "in your face"... :-)

Anyone using a Windows platform already has the VB and C++ runtimes, but 
only a tiny percentage of the user base would even know they are there. The 
C# "runtime" is already part of the OS Extensions in the form of the .Net 
Framework. The COBOL runtimes are much more obvious; if you use Micro Focus 
you pay a license fee for it, use Fujitsu and it is a separate install with 
anything you deploy elsewhere.

Pete.
```

```
installs for multiple combinations, you cannot run everything you want on
```

```
On Wed, 14 Dec 2011 14:14:59 -0800 (PST), Ken <klshafer@att.net>
wrote:

>On Dec 14, 8:35 am, "Pete Dashwood"
><dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[122 more quoted lines elided]…
>Ken

I think we have an issue of terminology here.  The University I went
to (Purdue) had (and still has as far as I know) separate degree paths
for Computer Science and Computer Technology.

The Computer Science majors, while they did learn some programming
languages, were more concerned with logic, switch theory and writing
compilers.  They delved into things like writing programs whose object
could be burned onto a micro chip and such things like that.

The Computer Technology people (I was one) learned all the languages
(at the time RPG, Assembler (optional) COBOL and Fortran.  You could
take courses depending on your discipline that taught you Assembler
and Fortran on a mini-computer.  You also took business courses, if
that was your discipline, or science courses, if that was your
discipline, or system (IBM) courses if you were looking to be a
System's Programmer.  As I wasn't sure what I wanted to do, I took
business and Systems courses and am damn glad I did even though it was
a ton more work.

Now I know that some community colleges and trade schools do not
differentiate between Computer Science and Computer Technology.  And
it is possible things are different in today's universities as well.
But I think Purdue (and MIT and Stanford) got it right splitting the
two out.

Regards,
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Java apps have most flaws, Cobol apps the least

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-12-19T11:56:35+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9l79d6Fi92U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<slrnje6uah.c31.rodo@seeteufel.bloerp.de> <880bb748-460f-4446-937e-3f32e8a55a2b@d10g2000vbf.googlegroups.com> <dd7c6a73-48be-431d-81df-85b30f8534b0@t16g2000vba.googlegroups.com> <9kn4h2Fc2dU1@mid.individual.net> <73fabb59-fc82-4c32-bf4b-323be45279cf@q11g2000vbq.googlegroups.com> <9krn0lF8kbU1@mid.individual.net> <4eed1847$0$3691$6e1ede2f@read.cnntp.org>`

```
L�ko Willms wrote:
> Am 14.12.2011 14:35, schrieb Pete Dashwood:
>> Hmmm.... Over a long career (more than 4 decades) working around the
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>   So you have begun your computer career in 1970 or earlier.

Your arithmetic is impeccable. :-)

I started programming in 1965 and moved to COBOL in 1967.

> Back then
> there was no way to get a degree in computing sciences.

Maybe not where you lived.

In NZ the University of Auckland offered a Bachelor of Science degree with
computing units as part of it. (these could be cross credited to a Commerce
degree at the time, also.) I worked with a guy who had done his BSc in
Auckland and learned Fortran as part of it. Another guy I knew at IBM had
done a similar degree with computing units in the South Island.

The Auckland Technical Institute (since renamed) offered COBOL courses about
the same time, along with general computing units for a Certificate. (I
forget exactly what the Certification was, but I knew several people who did
the course and worked with a girl who had completed it and who was one of
the brightest programmers I have ever known. Given that her major
achievement in our company was the optimization of a heuristic corrugator
scheduler  program written in Fortran (optimizing how the machine made cuts
in corrugated cardboard to minimize waste), I think it is fair to say that
she had a background in "computer science".

It was a few years later that people could actually enrol on a "Computing
Science" degree course. Unfortunately most of the graduates who came off it
were completely unsuited and unprepared for data processing, which is what
most computers at the time were doing. I worked with a guy who wrote a truly
amazng random number generator, (I used it for a a program I wrote for IBM
360 which simulated duck shooting), but he just fell apart when asked to
write a three file merge. Not his fault; Acadaemia was simply out of touch
with the marketplace. (Some would argue they stilll are to this day...)

By the early 1970s things had improved and we were getting graduates who
were not entirely useless. It was just as well because it was around that
time that CICS and IMS/DB/DC had established themselves and online
transaction processing was becoming very important. This needed
understanding of re-entrancy and re-usability which was quite a bit more
complex than simple batch processing.


>Those who did
> have a scientific background in computing were mathematicians,
> electrical engineers, physicists or what have you.

Mostly, yes, but not entirely. I had a Boss who had a computing science
degree around that time and he was a professional musician (classical
guitarist) before getting into computers in England, then emigrating to NZ.
>
>   University studies in "Informatik", as it is called in German, were
> not offered before the 1980ies.

By the 1980s our kids were doing it in what you would call "Gymnasium".
Today they start in the lower schools and use computers much the way we used
pencils.
>
>   So, having such a degree or not could not be of importance when you
> began (same for me, although my career started a decade later than
> yours).

No, it was nowhere near as "important" as it is today, but there were still
people who considered it worth doing.

A lot of this is about perception. HR departments love certificates because 
if the person is a screw-up they can say: "But we checked his/her 
qualifications and they were very good." I told you the story of the useless 
PhD I worked with for a while.

Paper is an easy option for recruiters who have no idea about what is 
actually required. There is no substitute for a personal interview and 
evaluation.

Having said all of that, I don't want to disparage the pursuit and 
attainment of a qualification. (In computing or anything else.)

The applicant is an end result of their life experience to date. Sometimes 
academic pusuit can be very beneficial in that regard as well as providing 
useful information that is applicable in the workplace, sometimes it is no 
value at all. It depends on the person and the study they undertook.

That's why I prefer to interview people myself (rather than let HR do it) 
and that's why I make results and attitude the two top point scoring 
attributes.

And on the other side of the coin, when I am applying for a job myself 
(hasn't happened for a very long time now and is unlikely to again) I 
present a documented track record, rather than certificates, and I am 
interested to hear what they are doing, what their problems are and suggest 
how I might contribute to helping them solve them. The latter is far more 
important than the former; my full CV is over 30 pages so there is usually 
something pertinient in it somewhere but I wouldn't expect anybody (except 
maybe the most chronic insomniac) to wade through all of it.

I had someone once say to me with a grin: "I read your CV..."

I replied: "Wait 'til the movie comes out... " :-)

>
>> I have met outstanding people in both categories, so I'm not sure
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> years of computing experience on their backs.

Well, if you can't, you better be prepared to train them.

Of course, Companies need young people who can be trained into their 
particular systems but you certainly can't run an effective development 
centre based on trainees. There need to be older, wiser, mentors who can 
help the newbies progress.

Very often (I found this particulary in Europe, not so much in the 
Antipodes) Companies WON'T train people because they are afraid that as soon 
as they do the people will move on. It is a specious argument; if you invest 
in people and recognise their increased value they usually reward you with 
loyalty. Why would anybody leave a place where they are valued and 
recognised and where their skills are encouraged to expand? Run a happy 
wrkplace and you won't have trouble retaining staff. (In fact you may well 
have people anxious to work for you...)
>
>   And in this situation, having a degree or being a "self made man"
> can make a difference. Unless the self made computer professional can
> point to some showcases of systems she or he has built being a
> student.

If they built a track record as a student, that implies they were studying
for something. Presumably a qualification in computing?

But that is not the ONLY way to be able to show experience, and produce
listings and documents of work you have done.

I believe, (and I have managed lots of teams of people) that experience can
count as much as paper. There are also "intangibles" like attitude and
personality that are not necessarily learned on a computing science course.

Like I said originally, in my opinion it is the results that matter. My
experience has not led me to believe that formal study produces better
results than the empirical approach. Both are valuable; both need to produce
results, but at the end of the day it is people who will achieve or fail. I
would (and have) employ someone with no qualifications and very little
experience, if their attitude and approach is right. But that's just me... I
realise that is not the norm.

Pete.
```

```
The following is (a slightly modified version of) what I posted on teh 
OpenCOBOL forum,

     Another new (free) COBOL available
I have just become aware of another (new I belive) FREE COBOL product. If 
you are interested in seeing it (and comparing it, check out:

https://www.elasticcobol.com/

The documentation on getting started is NOT very good. (I am still working 
my way thru it).

It seems to have two free options.

1) There is a 14-day trial which allows you to test the more extensive 
version. I think you just select one of the full products and "cancel" 
after/before 14 days. (This includes both SQL and CICS support)

2) The version that I am playing with runs on the Cloud (the IDE and the 
compiler and the executables ALL run on the cloud, not on your machien. The 
response time on this is medium slow, but it does seem to be VERY full 
featured (but not CICS or SQL - but does include HTML and some remote 
features)

Checking the LRM, this compiler accepts a WIDE range of extensions (most 
Micro Focus and IBM mainframe extensions, and a number that I have never 
seen before.  I think it has signficant COBOL/400 and HP COBOL language 
features)

So far, their support has been pretty good in helping me with "getting 
started".

This is NOT an OPEN SOURCE product. If you are doing "real work" with their 
product and go with the non-free version, the pricing scheme is interesting 
(to me). check it out at:

  https://www.elasticcobol.com/index.php/pricing/



Special note to those uinterested in OO COBOL:

  As far as I can tell the CLOUD (free) version has full OO COBOL support 
(both ISO 2002 and MF compatible) as well as "close" integration with Java 
(but not C#)
```

## Thread: Fujitsu Cobol and MS SQL Server — 9 msgs

### Fujitsu Cobol and MS SQL Server

- **From:** Zalek Bloom <zalekbloom@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-12-14T16:47:45-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,alt.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<c8ea7e8c-de46-4465-b0e8-64a6737bfe42@j10g2000vbe.googlegroups.com>`

```
I am using Fujitsu Cobol with MS SQL Server. In order to improve
performance our DBA suggested to insert the following statement to our
SQL statements:
set nocount on

My question: how to embad this statemnt in SQL?

When I tried

     EXEC SQL
         set nocount on
     END-EXEC.

I got a compile error saying: The SQL statemnt is invalid. Odbc-7600e
NOT EXECUTABLE STATEMENT

Any idea how to do it?

Thanks,

Zalek
```

#### ↳ Re: Fujitsu Cobol and MS SQL Server

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-12-14T22:58:32-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,alt.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<f79ce588-9d48-4d58-8823-a107dbff0487@r13g2000prr.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<c8ea7e8c-de46-4465-b0e8-64a6737bfe42@j10g2000vbe.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Dec 15, 1:47 pm, Zalek Bloom <zalekbl...@gmail.com> wrote:
> I am using Fujitsu Cobol with MS SQL Server. In order to improve
> performance our DBA suggested to insert the following statement to our
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> Any idea how to do it?

Fujitsu will only process the SQL statement defined in their manual,
it won't process random (but valid) SQL statements that are not
described in the manual.

While it may not be an invalid statement in SQL, Fujitsu does not know
how to action it.
```

```
I'm too busy at the moment to actually try this, but Fujitsu does support 
dynamic SQL very well (better than Micro Focus).

It should work with a string presented to EXECUTE IMMEDIATE, without 
actually needing PREPARE. If it doesn't, then it would certainly work as a 
stored procedure. Maybe the DBA should set up a suitable SP and let COBOL 
call it.

I'd try something along these lines:

define a Host Variable as a string...

...   01 SQLSTMT pic x(256).

...
       move "SET COUNT OFF" to SQLSTMT
       EXEC SQL
             EXECUTE IMMEDIATE :SQLSTMT
       END-EXEC

Pretty sure that will do it.

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Fujitsu Cobol and MS SQL Server

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-12-17T01:37:50+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,alt.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9l0sd0FhtlU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<c8ea7e8c-de46-4465-b0e8-64a6737bfe42@j10g2000vbe.googlegroups.com> <0fa10e12-128e-4759-bc94-fe4209133666@h11g2000yqd.googlegroups.com>`

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Dec 15, 12:47 am, Zalek Bloom <zalekbl...@gmail.com> wrote:
>> I am using Fujitsu Cobol with MS SQL Server. In order to improve
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> statement or is it a compiler directive or what?

"SET NOCOUNT ON" is a T-SQL statement provided for MS SQL Server.

It is part of SQL Server's programming language, Transact-SQL.

This language is an extension by Microsoft of the original SQL developed by 
IBM.

There are sites where they use T-SQL for all development (I managed one in 
the Midlands of England) and it is a fully fledged programming language that 
is complete in itself just like COBOL or VB.

There are some very powerful commands available.

Check "Transaction SQL" on Google.

When I first took over the site I mentioned, I was a bit shocked to find 
that the "programmers" were SQL guys. But I soon found out that they were 
very competent and had some amazing tools. I became persuaded when I saw how 
quickly they produced stuff. They could do in 10 minutes, using graphic drag 
and drop style tools, what would take three days to do in COBOL...

Although most ODBC drivers will not accept direct T-SQL commands it is 
pretty easy to execute them as dynamic SQL, as I showed elsewhere in this 
thread. (Always provided you are connected to MSSQL, of course... )

Probably worth mentioning that many systems connect directly to various 
databases without using ODBC, by means of ADO, OLEDB, or named pipe 
connections, so the limitations of ODBC do not apply to them. Nevertheless, 
ODBC is definitely the most flexible approach, even if not the most 
efficient. It is hard enough supporting Access, SQL Server, SQLite, MySQL, 
PostgreSQL,  Oracle and DB2 with the different syntax and idiosynchronisms 
of each of these platforms, without having to build a separate product for 
each of them. ODBC means you don't have to, but it is still non-trivial to 
get absolutely equivalent functionality across all platforms.

Fujitsu COBOL requires ODBC for embedded SQL (ESQL) and you cannot use 
resultsets from OLEDB or similar. (A SQL cursor is the way to do it in COBOL 
but it is the least favoured way outside COBOL; resultsets are many times 
more efficient.) Fujitsu PowerCOBOL offers an ADO control object or you can 
instantiate ADO as an Automation server and use it via COM. It is better 
than ESQL but not as good as LINQ. I've never tried experimenting with OLEDB 
from COBOL but in theory it should be possible to use it just like ADO if 
you instantiate an instance of it and manipulate its methods with OO COBOL.

The embedded procedural SQL which a large percentage of the world's COBOL 
programs implement is, frankly, obsolete.

C#  can use LINQ and Lambda expressions which are just a quantum leap above 
ESQL. (I understand this will be made available to VB.Net in the near 
future.)

The SQL which runs on modern platforms is a very long way away from the 8 
basic commands which ran in the early 1980s...

(SELECT, INSERT, UPDATE, DELETE, DECLARE, OPEN, FETCH, CLOSE)

However, there are many COBOL programmers who believe that 8 commands are 
all there is and definitely all you need... :-)

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Fujitsu Cobol and MS SQL Server

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Fujitsu Cobol and MS SQL Server

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-12-18T18:24:44+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,alt.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9l5bouFqvU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<c8ea7e8c-de46-4465-b0e8-64a6737bfe42@j10g2000vbe.googlegroups.com> <0fa10e12-128e-4759-bc94-fe4209133666@h11g2000yqd.googlegroups.com> <9l0sd0FhtlU1@mid.individual.net> <9d443f36-8544-4e98-ae4a-a8974ebfdcb1@y18g2000yqy.googlegroups.com>`

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Dec 16, 12:37 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> Real programmers know that you also need DROP and CREATE.

As you may be aware the blanket of "SQL" really falls onto two beds:

1. Data Definition Language (DDL) which also includes "Administrative" 
statements such as GRANT and REVOKE as well as CREATE, DROP, and ALTER.

(Passing note: When you create tables with the PRIMA Toolset you can see the 
generated DDL by pulling down the DDL directory from the Files, menu. The 
Toolset generates the DDL then runs it automatically.)

2. Data Manipulation Language (DML) which includes the 8 statements noted 
above, as well as a few others which may be vendor dependent.

Because most implementations of ODBC only allow DML statements, this subset 
has become known loosely as "SQL". Probably it should be called ESQL because 
Embedded SQL is almost invariably DML only.
>
> Thanks to Pete and Pavel for their responses. I am a bit miffed that
> neither of the teo thick books I used as references mentioned SET
> NOCOUNT ON so that is a good fifty quid wasted.

Glad to help.

You may be being a little unfair, Alistair. It is a SQL Server only 
statement. If either of your books was SQL Server specific, I'm pretty sure 
you'd find it.
>
> Thanks guys.

Pete.
```

---

<a id="t-b51d436017"></a>
## Thread: guicobol has been moved to a opensource — 1 msgs

### guicobol has been moved to a opensource

- **From:** federico priolo <priolof@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-12-15T22:35:19-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<f0bcbd17-d426-4c57-ab1e-ec741738593b@cs7g2000vbb.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hi all, the project I am working on is going ahead. I moved the
guicobol tool to an opensource license and then you can try it.

The guicobol is a preprocessor that wants to give a gui interface to
opencobol 1.1 by using the gtk opensource libraries. It realizes this
using an object like syntax.

The project and documents are available at www.tp-srl.it

here a sample demo...

000010  IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
000020  PROGRAM-ID.    label1.
000030  ENVIRONMENT DIVISION.
000040  DATA DIVISION.
000050  WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.
000060
000150  01 olabel               usage pointer external.
000150  01 obutton             usage pointer external.
000210
000220           copy "global".
000230
000240  PROCEDURE DIVISION.
000260
000270           invoke gtk-form "createform"
000280
000290           move "Label sample" to "title" of self.
000300
000320           move 800    to "width" of gtk-form.
000330           move 220    to "height" of gtk-form.
000740
000750           invoke gtk-form "createlabel" using olabel
000760             "move the mouse on the screen " line 1 pos 1

                     move 200    to "width" of olabel.
000910
000350           invoke self "createbutton" using obutton
000360                "Exit" line 180 pos 10

                     move 100    to "width" of obutton.

                    move "This enable the move.." to "tooltip" of
obutton.

000940          invoke obutton  "clicked" using "click"

                     invoke gtk-form "mousemoved" using "mousemoved".

001020           invoke gtk-form "showform".

001030
001040           invoke gtk-form "run".
001050
001080           stop run.
001090
001100  end program label1.
001110
001380  identification division.
001390  program-id. click.
001400  data division.
001410  working-storage section.
001500
001510      copy "global".
001530
001540  linkage section.

001550  01 gtk-window           usage pointer.
001560  01 gtk-data             usage pointer.
001570
001580  procedure division using by value gtk-window by value gtk-
data.
001590
002290            invoke gtk-form "stoprun".


001680  exit program.
001690  end program click.
001700
002520
002120  identification division.
002130  program-id. mousemoved.
002140  data division.

002150  working-storage section.

000130  01 olabel             usage pointer external.

002200      copy "global".

001880 linkage section.
001890 01 gtk-window           usage pointer.
001900 01 gtk-data             usage pointer.
001910
001920 procedure division using by value gtk-window
                                by value gtk-data.


             invoke gtk-form "get-pointer" using gtk-x gtk-y

             invoke olabel "moveto" using gtk-x gtk-y.

002320  exit program.
002330  end program mousemoved.
```

## Thread: Offtopic: nntp.olduse.net — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Web, XML, modern integration`](topics.md#web) · [`Off-topic and spam`](topics.md#spam)_

### Offtopic: nntp.olduse.net

- **From:** Marko <notformail@rediffmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-12-21T18:30:29+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<jct53q$bp6$1@dont-email.me>`

```
Hello Cobolians,

<delurk>
although I have never written a single line of Cobol, I'm browsing this 
group since a couple of years. I appreciate the experience (and 
sometimes even wisdom) of some of you. Seems a lot of you are my age or 
even older :-)
</delurk>

Don't know if you already know: There is some kind of time warp project 
on nntp.olduse.net (http://olduse.net/), kicking us 30 years back in 
usenet. It's a unique historical (electronical) archive of the very 
early (early at least for me) days. My personal favourite: "current 
usenet map" on net.general, 14.06.1981.

Just thought maybe it could be interesting for some of you...

Marko
```

```
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> I don't know even one Sheldon Cooper, let alone several hundred million.

A joke explained ...

Sheldon Cooper is a fictional character on the CBS television
series "The Big Bang Theory". Sheldon is an intellectually
gifted, narcissistic, obsessive-compulsive, multi-phobic
theoritical physicist, who has not yet won a Nobel Prize;
but is convinced he will, some day.

To muse about any number of Sheldon Coopers, is to amuse
or terrify, your choice.

OTOH, Dr. George Smoot who "won the Nobel Prize in Physics
in 2006 for his work on COBE with John C. Mather that led
to the measurement 'of the black body form and anisotropy
of the cosmic microwave background radiation'" had a guest
appearance in the episode "The Terminator Decoupling",
where his line to Sheldon was, as I recall, "Are you on
crack!"
```

<a id="t-47c5ffabba"></a>
## Thread: Converting old IBM code to ANSI 85 — 18 this month / 29 total

_Topics: [`COBOL standards (ANS, ISO, 74/85/2002/2014)`](topics.md#standards) · [`Migration and conversion`](topics.md#migration)_

### Converting old IBM code to ANSI 85

- **From:** Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-12-29T13:12:03-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1cd2a4b3-c242-40a4-a3a4-68129794fbbd@z19g2000vbe.googlegroups.com>`

```
I am entering a program from a source listing for conversion
from IBM to Micro Focus. The SELECT clause is not compatible
with ANSI. Following are, what I believe, the essential parts
of the source for one problem.

...
       date-written. 12/25/76.
       date-compiled. 03/14/78.
...
       source-computer. ibm-360-f40.
       object-computer. ibm-360-f40.
       input-output section.
       file-control.
           select work-file assign to sys001-da-2314-d-ijsysxx
               access mode is random
               actual key is key-relative.
       i-o-control.
       data division.
       file section.
       fd  work-file recording mode is f
                   record contains 1250 characters
                   label records are standard
                   data record is work-rec.
       01  work-rec                    pic x(1250).
       working-storage section.
       01  key-relative.
           03  key-rel                 pic s9(9) comp.
           03  key-rel-x               redefines key-rel
                                       pic x(4).
           03  key-rel-key             pic x(4).
...
       write-disk.
           move rel-key-x to key-rel-key.
           compute key-rel = key-rel / 5.
           write work-rec from i-o-buffers (blk-id)
               invalid key display msg-1 upon console
               go to abort-job.
       xwrite-disk.
       read-disk.
           move rel-key-x to key-rel-key.
           compute key-rel = key-rel / 5.
           read work-file into i-o-buffers (blk-id)
               invalid key display msg-2 upon console
               go to abort-job.
       xread-disk.
...

According to GC28-6403-0 "IBM System/360 Disk Operating System
Subset American National Standard COBOL", organization "d" is
for direct files with relative track addressing. Then, in the
"actual key" clause, "key-relative" contains "key-rel" (the
relative track number) and "key-rel-key" (the record identifier).

Furthermore, the "key-rel / 5" in "write-disk" and "read-disk"
appears to limit the number of records per "2314" track to 5,
which seems about right from my memory (7200 / 1250 = 5.76).

Now, if I have done my "homework" correctly, the changes will
be:

a. Redo the SELECT clause for ANSI relative files with
   "relative key is key-rel".
b. Remove the references to "key-rel-x" and "key-rel-key".
c. Eliminate the COMPUTE statement in "write-disk" and
   "read-disk".

It is likely to be much more than a week before I complete
the entering of the source code; but I thought I would ask
for comments on this problem. So, any comments?
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Converting old IBM code to ANSI 85

- **Message-ID:** `<jdkbt9$iu6$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
In article <f52bdb82-5a59-437d-88e5-7dcab2732d65@d8g2000vbb.googlegroups.com>,
Rick Smith  <rs847925@gmail.com> wrote:
>On Dec 29, 8:07?pm, docdw...@panix.com () wrote:

[snip]

>> My next step would be to determine the transaction volume handled by this
>> program and see if changes in technology in the past
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>intended use.

That's a possibility, of course.  Assuming that this 3000-card input file 
is used mostly for lookups a way I've dealt with that in the past was to 
sort the file and load it into an Occurs Depending On table in the 
HOUSKEEPING section.

SEARCH ALL and you're on your way... and the table load routines are built 
so that when you get close to your limit the program starts to throw 
messages to show that the volume being passed might require redesign.

DD
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Converting old IBM code to ANSI 85

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-12-30T09:43:22-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<CqWdneeuh7yBQWDTnZ2dnUVZ_jWdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **References:** `<1cd2a4b3-c242-40a4-a3a4-68129794fbbd@z19g2000vbe.googlegroups.com> <jdj2sf$hb3$1@reader1.panix.com> <f52bdb82-5a59-437d-88e5-7dcab2732d65@d8g2000vbb.googlegroups.com>`

```
Rick Smith wrote:
>>
>> This appears to be an appropriate remediation.
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> intended use.

ISAM (or equivalent) will indeed be appropriate. Some may root for SEARCH or 
SEARCH ALL, but you may still reach an internal limit on array sizes. Plus, 
with ISAM, advances in operating systems and compilers will be doing the 
work in memory anyway. In other words, there won't be the overhead of disk 
access that was common on 60's era computers.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Converting old IBM code to ANSI 85

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-12-30T10:54:17-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
On Dec 30, 10:43 am, "HeyBub" <hey...@NOSPAMgmail.com> wrote:
> Rick Smith wrote:

[snip]

> > There was never more than 3000 cards input. The program appears to
> > use the work file only for table overflow. The program ran in a 96Kb
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> access that was common on 60's era computers.

The current program does not save the output -- it prints a
report. For my intended use and after some adaptations, I want
to save the output for use in a program yet to be considered
and think ISAM would be a good choice for that purpose.

I have moved tables into files, both relative and indexed, in
production programs on PCs, without any complaints of degradation
in performance; so I know of what you speak. However, this is
not a production program, as such, and sometimes it is simply
more fun to do the exotic. <g>
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Converting old IBM code to ANSI 85

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>

- **From:** seasoned_geek <roland@logikalsolutions.com>
- **Date:** 2011-12-31T08:37:29-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8e8767bb-0380-46bf-8c3f-60a15a1fcd0a@f33g2000yqh.googlegroups.com>`

```
"The Minimum You Need to Know to Be an OpenVMS Application Developer"
ISBN-13 978-0-9770866-3-4
is now available in EPUB form from Sony.

http://ebookstore.sony.com/ebook/roland-hughes/the-minimum-you-need-to-know-to-be-an-openvms-application-developer/_/R-400000000000000570154

You can download the original CD content from
http://www.theminimumyouneedtoknow.com/app_book.html

Those in foreign countries who wanted this title but could not justify
the $30+ in shipping for such a large book can now purchase it for
your eReader.
```
